1001 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



United Farm Workers v. Agricultural Labor Relations Bd.


No. H009259


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE


16 Cal. App. 4th 1629; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 879; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 698; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5070; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8482



June 30, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 17 ALRB No. 16



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Agricultural Labor Relations Board is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner labor union appealed from an order of respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board (California), entered after remand, finding that although defendant grower's refusal to bargain violated the Agricultural Labor Relations Act, make-whole relief was not appropriate.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board (the Board) initially found defendant grower guilty of refusal to bargain in violation of the Agricultural Labor Relations Act, and ordered make-whole relief for his employees, pursuant to Cal. Lab. § 1160.3. After interim proceedings, and on remand, respondent ruled that make-whole relief was inappropriate, because defendant had shown that his failure to reach agreement with petitioner union was not a result of a refusal to bargain, but resulted from legitimate and independent economic causes. Petitioner sought review, and the court affirmed. The court found that substantial evidence had been presented that the parties' central disagreement was based on petitioner's insistence that defendant pay wage rates comparable to those petitioner had obtained from another grower in another county; that growers based in defendant's county, in direct competition with defendant, paid substantially lower wage rates; that it would not be economically feasible for defendant to pay the rates demanded; and that defendant thus demonstrated that the parties would not have agreed, even if the employer had not refused to bargain.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed. Respondent Agricultural Labor Relations Board properly found that make-whole relief was not appropriate, because defendant grower's failure to reach an agreement with appellant labor union was not caused by defendant's refusal to bargain but, resulted from the parties' independent inability to agree on wage rates.

 

1002 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


San Luis Coastal Unified Sch. Dist. v. Kunkel


No. B066612


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX.


16 Cal. App. 4th 1407; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 724; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 690; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5008; 93 Daily Journal DAR 8530



June 29, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 30, 1993 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5287.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 70241, Leighton Hatch, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Sacramento Municipal Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Respondents to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which found that respondent, San Luis Coastal Unified School District's, measure, authorizing respondent to use a pre-Proposition 13 tax override to pay for the lease purchase of new facilities, was proper under Cal. Educ. Code § 39308 and did not violate Proposition 13, Cal. Const. art. XIIIA, §1.


OVERVIEW: Before Proposition 13, Cal. Const., art. XIII A, § 1, was passed, respondent San Luis Coastal Unified School District's voters approved a measure for the lease-purchase of three school sites. After Proposition 13 and after the tax levy had been reduced to zero, the electorate authorized respondent to use the original tax override to pay the lease-purchase debt for more new facilities. Respondent filed an action in the district court, seeking to validate the measure and appellant taxpayers challenged the action, contending that the taxes would violate Proposition 13, which limited ad valorem taxation to one percent of the value of the project. The trial court disagreed, and the court affirmed, finding that the proposed tax override was based on a "prior indebtedness" that was not subject to Proposition 13. Because the court found that the earlier lease obligation was not yet paid and discharged and that the new taxes were for the same basic purpose as the original taxes, the "prior indebtedness" exception, codified in Cal. Educ. Code § 39308.5, allowed respondent to use a continuing tax override for the new facilities. Such application of § 39308.5 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, finding that respondent San Luis Coastal Unified School District's tax override was a "prior indebtedness" that had not been discharged, that such "prior indebtedness" fell under the exception to Proposition 13's one-percent limitation on ad valorem taxes, and that such application of the exception statute was not unconstitutional.

 

1003 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Quiroga


No. A058769


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DIVISION ONE


16 Cal. App. 4th 961; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 446; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 653; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4639; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7881



June 22, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. *]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1., this opinion is certified for partial publication. The portions directed to be published follow.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied September 30, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5279.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. CR09647, Eric Labowitz, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Justice Court for the Anderson Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which convicted him after a jury trial for charges of possession of cocaine under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(a) and resisting a peace officer under Cal. Pen. Code § 148 when he did not give his identity during the booking process after his arrest.


OVERVIEW: Police were called regarding noise from a party where defendant was present. After police entered the party, they smelled marijuana. Defendant stood up from a couch but the officers ordered him to sit back down. The officers found a bag of what was later confirmed to be cocaine. After defendant was arrested, he refused to give his name during the booking process. His identity was later confirmed. There was an outstanding warrant for a drug charge but reference to that warrant was limited. Defendant was tried and convicted for possession of cocaine under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(a) and resisting a peace officer under Cal. Pen. Code § 148. Defendant contended that since the latter charge should not have been brought because he contended that his behavior did not constitute resisting a police officer, and as such, evidence regarding the outstanding warrant should not have been introduced. The court affirmed the lower court's conviction because defendant's refusal to disclose his identity at the booking interview served to resist, delay, and obstruct the responsible peace officer in the discharge of his duties and thus, fell under the realm of Cal. Penal Code § 148.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's conviction of defendant for possession of cocaine and resisting a peace officer because defendant consistently refused to give his identity during the booking process, which frustrated the process and was in violation of the state statute pertaining to resisting a peace officer.

 

1004 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Hecht v. Superior Court


No. B073747


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


16 Cal. App. 4th 836; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 275; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 638; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4531; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7656



June 17, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Real Party in Interest's Review Denied September 2, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4768.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BP012006, Edward M. Ross, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the superior court to (1) vacate its January 4, 1993, order, (2) enter a new order denying the "Petition for Alternative Relief [Instructions to Destroy Decedent's Frozen Sperm or Order for Preliminary Distribution of Said Sperm and Order of Determination of Whether Children Conceived from Said Sperm Shall Be Entitled to Distribution and Instructions Concerning Administration of Estate Pending Conception and Birth of Said Children]," and (3) conduct further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein. Upon compliance with this writ, our order staying execution of the January 4, 1993, order is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, girlfriend of decedent, sought a peremptory writ of mandamus to vacate the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that ordered the personal representative of decedent's estate to destroy all of decedent's sperm in the custody and control of a sperm bank at the request of the real parties in interest, decedent's personal representative and adult children.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, decedent's girlfriend, sought review after the lower court entered an order requiring that decedent's sperm be destroyed. The court reversed the order finding that it had been entered prematurely because the validity of decedent's will and his contract with the sperm bank, both of which contained instructions that petitioner was entitled to the sperm and that she could become impregnated with it if she so chose, had not been determined. The real parties in interest, decedent's personal representative and adult children, argued that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 7054.4, which addressed destruction of human tissue, required that the sperm be destroyed. The court found that § 7054.4 was not intended to address issues relating to sperm. The court concluded that at the time of his death, decedent had an interest in his sperm, because he had decision making authority regarding it, and that this interest was sufficient to constitute property within the meaning of Cal. Prob. Code § 62, and as such, the probate court had jurisdiction over the vials of sperm. The court found that artificial insemination with the sperm of a decedent did not violate public policy.


OUTCOME: The court granted the peremptory writ of mandate and directed the lower court to vacate its order requiring that decedent's sperm be destroyed, enter an order denying the request to destroy the sperm, and conduct further proceedings finding that it was an abuse of discretion to order the sperm destroyed without first addressing the validity of decedent's will and his contract with the sperm bank, both of which contained disposition instructions.

 

1005 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


In re Bettencourt


No. H010325


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


16 Cal. App. 4th 722; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 240; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 623; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4460; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7559



June 15, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 30, 1993 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5298.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 678646, Nancy Hoffman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: There is no substantial evidence that Bettencourt had the ability to comply with the court orders underlying this contempt proceeding. The order of contempt is annulled.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father sought a writ of habeas corpus after respondent Superior Court of Santa Clara County(California) found petitioner in criminal contempt when he did not pay child support, medical insurance, and attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Respondent trial court issued an order finding petitioner father in contempt for failing to pay child support, medical insurance, and attorney fees. Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus and alleged that respondent had no jurisdiction to render the order as there was not substantial evidence that petitioner could make the payments. The court granted the petition and annulled the contempt order. Petitioner presented evidence that he made insufficient money to make the payments at issue. The appellate court concluded that the respondent's decision to disbelieve petitioner's evidence of his inability to pay was erroneous. No evidence was presented to controvert petitioner's contention that he could not pay. Respondent also inferred petitioner's ability to pay from his earlier stipulation to an increase in child support payments. However, the court stated that this was only circumstantial evidence and that a criminal contempt conviction based on circumstantial evidence required circumstances to be entirely consistent with the theory of guilt. Here, the child support order did not necessarily lead to the conclusion that petitioner had the ability to pay.


OUTCOME: The appellate court granted the writ of habeas corpus and annulled the contempt order because there was not substantial evidence that petitioner had the ability to comply with respondent trial court's order to pay child support, medical insurance, and attorney fees.

 

1006 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Planned Parenthood v. City of Santa Maria


No. B063569


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX


16 Cal. App. 4th 685; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 391; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 626; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4447; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7538



June 15, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing July 13, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 725.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. SM69374, Zel Canter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The parties shall bear their own attorney fees and costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff clinic appealed the decision by the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which refused to order the payment of grant money and private attorney general fees to plaintiff after determining that defendant city's conditioning of grant money on the non-performance of abortions was improper. Defendant cross-appealed the decision that the condition was improper.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city approved plaintiff clinic's grant application upon the condition that no abortions be performed at the clinic. Plaintiff protested the condition and filed a claim for declaratory and injunctive relief and private attorney general fees. The trial court concluded that the condition violated the Cal. Const., but did not order defendant to turn over grant funds. On appeal, plaintiff contended that the trial court should have ordered the turnover of the grant money. However, the appellate court concluded that because plaintiff did not request that relief in its complaint the trial court correctly refused to take that extra step. In addition, the trial court's refusal to order payment of private attorney general fees was affirmed as the constitutional privacy right issue was incidental to the plaintiff's primary objective of obtaining grant money. The court rejected defendant's argument on cross-appeal that the abortion restriction was lawful. Defendant was not permitted to condition receipt of federal funds upon the waiver of a state constitutional right. Federal law did not place any restrictions on the distribution of funds and did not require preemption of state laws.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial court's decision that plaintiff clinic was not entitled to the grant money because plaintiff did not request that relief in its complaint, nor was plaintiff entitled to private attorney general fees as the claim was primarily to obtain the grant money. The court denied defendant city's cross-appeal because preemption of state constitutional rights was not required in the administration of grant funds.

 

1007 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. King


No. A058038


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


16 Cal. App. 4th 567; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 220; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 606; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4378; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7415



June 11, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 1292017, Donna Alyson Little, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Conceding some disproportionality,


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged part of his sentence from the Municipal Court of the City and County of San Francisco, (California), which required him to be registered as a sex offender, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 290, following a misdemeanor conviction of indecent exposure under Cal. Penal Code § 314(1).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of the misdemeanor indecent exposure in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 314(1). He challenged that part of his sentence that required him to register as a sex offender, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 290. Appellant contended that as a matter of law, the sex offender registration requirement constituted cruel and unusual punishment when applied to a misdemeanor conviction of indecent exposure. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court concluded that appellant's individual culpability justified imposition of a registration requirement. The court found that appellant failed to produce any evidence, as was his burden, that the registration punishment prescribed for his offense was disproportionate to that imposed for more serious offenses in California or for similar offenses in other jurisdictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court concluded that appellant's individual culpability justified imposition of a registration requirement.

 

1008 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Granados


No. B067804


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


16 Cal. App. 4th 517; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 131; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 603; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4326; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7350



June 10, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 15, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4849.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA048326, Carol J. Fieldhouse, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), entered upon his plea of no contest to possession of money or instruments in excess of $ 100,000 involved in the unlawful sale or purchase of cocaine, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11370.6(a). Prior to his plea, the trial court denied defendant's motion to declare Cal Health & Safety Code § 11370.6 unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment entered upon appellant's plea of no contest to possession of money or instruments in excess of $ 100,000 involved in the unlawful sale or purchase of cocaine, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11370.6(a). The court rejected appellant's claim that Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11370.6 was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV. Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11370.6(b) provided that the state must prove that an attorney accepted a fee to represent a client accused under § 11370.6(a) with the intent to participate in the unlawful conduct or to disguise or aid in disguising the source of the funds or nature of the criminal activity. The court held that § 11370.6 did not impermissibly distinguish between attorneys and nonattorneys because no fundamental liberty interest or suspect classification was involved. The court also held that the distinction between attorneys and nonattorneys served and was rationally related to the legitimate state interest of avoiding any chilling effect upon the freedom to retain counsel of one's choice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment accepting appellant's plea of no contest to possession of money or instruments involved in the unlawful sale or purchase of cocaine. The statute was not unconstitutional on its face. The distinction between attorneys and nonattorneys involved no fundamental liberty interest or suspect classification and served the legitimate state interest of avoiding a chilling effect upon the right to counsel.

 

1009 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Looney v. Superior Court


No. B073886


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


16 Cal. App. 4th 521; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 182; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 611; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4384; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7400



June 10, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Real Party in Interest's Review Denied September 2, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4769.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. BC040311, BC044339, BC044700, BC044726, and BC041550, Raymond Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. A peremptory writ shall issue directing the trial court to vacate its order of January 14, 1993, denying petitioners' motion under section 425.13 on the ground of untimeliness and to conduct further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 36, plaintiffs sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to consider and rule on the merits of their motion for leave to amend their medical malpractice complaint to include punitive damages under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 425.13, which the trial court denied finding that it was time-barred.


OVERVIEW: Defendant real parties in interest moved to strike petitioner plaintiff's punitive damages claim on a medical negligence action for failing to comply with Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 425.13, which required the motion to be brought no less than nine months before the date the matter was first set for trial. Petitioner was a Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 36 plaintiff, which allowed terminally ill parties to bring accelerated actions so that they might live long enough to see the action to completion. The trial court found the motion time-barred by § 425.13. The court ordered the trial court to consider and rule on the merits of the motion and found that the two statutes were in conflict. The court had to give effect to both statutes without reaching an absurd or anomalous result. The court found that requiring § 36 plaintiffs to give up their punitive damages claim under § 425.13 in return for getting the action accelerated would have been an absurd result, so the court found an implied exception to § 425.13, which allowed § 36 plaintiffs to have their motion for leave to amend ruled on by the court, despite being within the nine month period under § 425.13.


OUTCOME: The court issued petitioner plaintiff's writ of mandate and directed respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County to vacate its order denying petitioner's motion for leave to amend as untimely filed under § 425.13, because the two statutes were in conflict the court implied an exception to §425.13 that permitted the granting of the motion for § 36 plaintiffs because to find otherwise would have led to an absurd and clearly anomalous result.

 

1010 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Arcadia Redevelopment Agency v. Ikemoto


No. B057768


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


16 Cal. App. 4th 444; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 112; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 602; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4262; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7267



June 9, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 25, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4468.


PRIOR HISTORY: Proceeding in mandamus. Super. Ct. No. S020454.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of mandate is denied. Costs are awarded to respondents.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner redevelopment agencies filed a petition for writ of mandamus and requested an immediate stay against respondent taxing authorities. The California Supreme Court transferred the matter to the court, which denied the request for a stay, but found good cause for review of the issue. The trial court again transferred the case to the court to reconsider its ruling in light of a new senate bill.


OVERVIEW: The court denied petitioner redevelopment agencies' request for a writ of mandamus challenging the implementation of Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 97 and related sections. The court held that Cal. Const. art. XVI, § 16 did not prevent the legislature from altering the levying and collection of taxation on redevelopment project property in a manner consistent with which it altered such on other property. The court held that respondent property tax collectors substantially complied with Cal. Gov't Code § 907 in giving notice to petitioners and that mere threats to withhold funds were not a violation of § 907. The court determined that Senate Bill No. 1559, 1992 Cal. Stat. 697, amended Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 97.5 to include community redevelopment agencies and to subject them to the apportionment of property tax revenues. The court also held that the legislature indicated plainly that it intended to clarify rather than change its enactment and that the specificity of the statute concerning the dates and applicability of the statutes supported the conclusion that the legislature intended Senate Bill No. 1559 to apply retroactively.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of mandamus and awarded costs to respondents. The court concluded that the legislature violated no constitutional or statutory provision in enacting a section regarding administrative fees of the California Revenue and Taxation Code and interpreted the statute passed to clarify the provisions to apply retroactively.

 

1011 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Conservatorship of Cooper


No. F018012


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE


16 Cal. App. 4th 414; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 84; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 599; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4282; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7264



June 8, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 231454, Donald B. Cantwell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed, and the matters are remanded to the trial court to settle the various accounts in accord with the directions of Probate Code section 2942, subdivision (c).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public guardian sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which disallowed appellant's claim for bond fees under Cal. Prob. Code § 2942(c).


OVERVIEW: A trial judge settled accounts in conservatorship proceedings, ordering that appellant public guardian was surcharged in whole or in part for a bond fee, which was charged by appellant and deposited in the county treasury in various individual estates under Cal. Prob. Code § 2942(c). Appellant sought review of the trial court's judgment. The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded the matters to the trial court to settle the various accounts in accord with the directions of § 2942(c). The court found that respondent objectors did not satisfactorily explain how the statute was constitutionally infirm. The court found that even though the county's cost of "doing business" was disproportionately greater than the cost of the official bond, the legislature showed a rational basis for allowing the public guardian a fee in each guardianship estate. The court determined that respondents failed to show manifest abuse in the statutory scheme for appellant's claim for bond fees. The court held, therefore, that the plain language of the statute directed the trial court to allow appellant the bond fee and did not commit the decision to the trial court's discretion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a trial court's judgment disallowing appellant public guardian's bond fees because the plain language of the California Probate Code directed the trial court to allow appellant the fee and did not commit the decision to the trial court's discretion. The court found that the Probate Code section was not constitutionally infirm. The court remanded to the trial court to settle the various accounts in accord with the Probate Code.

 

1012 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Barnes v. State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co.


No. B056809


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


16 Cal. App. 4th 365; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 87; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 608; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4380; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7222



June 8, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angles County, No. CA001131, Barnet M. Cooperman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed. Costs on appeal to State Farm.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insured challenged an order from the Superior Court of Los Angles County (California), which sustained respondent insurer's demurrer and dismissed his suit to preclude respondent from spending premium revenues on political activities opposed by him, and to compel respondent to distribute excess surplus funds to its policyholders under Cal. Const. art. I, § 2, Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.05(a) and Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.10(a).


OVERVIEW: In appellant insured's suit to preclude respondent insurer from spending premium revenues on political activities opposed by him, and to compel respondent to distribute excess surplus funds, the trial court sustained respondent's demurrer and dismissed appellant's suit. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding appellant did not state a cause of action for relief. Respondent's right of free speech was protected by Cal. Const. art. I, § 2, and appellant's rights as a dissenting policyholder were not impermissibly subordinated to those of respondent. Appellant was not compelled to purchase insurance from respondent and had no right to any surplus until respondent decided to declare a dividend. The court held that respondent was attacking respondent's director's decision regarding the declaration of a dividend, and failed to allege facts showing the directors' decision was not made in good faith or in the best interests of the corporation as required under the business judgment rule. The court held that appellant had not exhausted his administrative remedies under Cal. Ins. Code § 1861.05(a) and Cal. Ins. Code §1861.10(a) prior to bringing suit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant insured's suit against respondent insurer. Appellant failed to state a cause of action for relief in that he sought to preclude respondent from spending premium revenues on political activities opposed by him, and sought to compel respondent to distribute surplus funds. Appellant's right to free speech was not subordinated to respondent's and appellant failed to exhaust his administrative remedies.

 

1013 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Keating


No. B067329.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


31 Cal. App. 4th 1688; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 899; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 584; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4088; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6934



June 3, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected July 1, 1993. Review Granted September 30, 1993 (S033855), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 5268.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court. (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 16 Cal. App. 4th 280 and 21 Cal. App. 4th 1109 and 26 Cal. App. 4th 1613)] Review Dismissed March 23, 1995 and cause remanded to Ct. App., 2nd Dist., Div. 1 (rule 29.4(c), Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 2140.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BA025236, Lance A. Ito, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to reflect consecutive sentences of one year on counts 4, 8, 10, 11, 12, 15, 18, and 20, with sentence in excess of five years (i.e., three years) ordered stayed. Judgment on counts 2, 3, 5, 6, 13, 14, 16, and 17 is modified to reflect concurrent middle terms of three years. The aggregate state prison term remains at 10 years. As modified, the judgment is affirmed. The trial court is directed to send a corrected abstract of judgment to the Department of Corrections.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction entered in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), after a jury returned guilty verdicts on 17 separate counts of the crime of securities fraud in violation of Cal. Corp. Code § 25401. Defendant alleged that aiding and abetting the violation of Cal. Corp. Code § 25401 did not exist as a criminal offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on 17 separate counts of the crime of violation of Cal. Corp. Code § 25401 as an aider and abettor. Defendant appealed on the grounds that aiding and abetting of such a Corporations Code crime did not statutorily exist. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the principle under Cal. Penal Code § 31 of aiding and abetting was not new, and that defendant's participation in the offense was sufficient to prove his knowledge of the wrongdoing and his general intent. The court concluded that the state had proven a pattern consistent with criminal conduct whereby factual representations were made to the purchasers of debentures which were inaccurate and misleading--which representations defendant knew to be false and unfounded while contemporaneously failing to impart any negative information which, if known to the individual investors, would have provided them the basis for an informed decision. The court ruled that it would not disturb the verdict on appeal, and that there was no juror misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the trial court to reflect an aggregate state prison term of 10 years. As modified, the judgment was affirmed.

 

1014 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Matthews


No. F017310


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


16 Cal. App. 4th 102; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 801; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 570; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4154; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6986



May 28, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted August 19, 1993 (S033650), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4413.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 12722, John P. Moran, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, resident of Oregon, challenged the order of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which denied his petition for certificate of rehabilitation and pardon because defendant had not met the California residency requirement. Defendant appealed on grounds that the residency requirement of Cal. Penal Code § 4852.01(c), violated the equal protection clauses of the U.S. constitution and the state constitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to second degree burglary in February 1966, and was sentenced to two years probation. After successfully completing probation in March 1969, defendant was granted dismissal of the charge pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1203.4. In June 1971, defendant moved from California to Oregon. In December 1991, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 4852.01 et seq., defendant, as a resident of Oregon, filed a petition for certificate of rehabilitation and pardon in Tulare County Superior Court, which was denied because defendant wasn't a resident of California for three years prior to the filing of the petition. Defendant appealed on grounds that the residency requirement violated the equal protection clauses of the U.S. and California constitutions. The court found that there was a rational basis to limit the petitions to residents, and that there was a legitimate state interest in limiting the benefits of pardon to residents as an enhancement of employability and productivity of its residents. The court affirmed the trial court and held that the residency requirement was bona fide, appropriately defined, uniformly applied, and did not violate the equal protection clauses.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because the trial court properly denied defendant's petition for certificate of rehabilitation and pardon by applying a three year statutory residency requirement which did not violate the equal protection clauses of the federal and California constitutions.

 

1015 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Ehrlich v. City of Culver City


No. B055523


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


15 Cal. App. 4th 1737; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 468; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 552; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3839; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6574



May 25, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted March 16, 1995 (S033642), Reported at: 1995 Cal. LEXIS 1959.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C730079, John Zebrowski, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed to the extent the trial court ordered the City to vacate the $ 280,000 mitigation fee and awarded the developer attorney fees. The judgment is affirmed to the extent the trial court denied the petition for writ of mandate as to the lieu art fee. The trial court is ordered to vacate its judgment (as revised) and to vacate the peremptory writ and enter a new and different judgment denying in full the petition for writ of mandate. There has been no appeal from the sanctions order and that order is unaffected by this appeal. The matter is remanded to the trial court to determine whether, and to what extent, a lien on the property should be reinstated and for other further proceedings consistent with this opinion. The City is awarded its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a city and the city council, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which invalidated the imposition of a development mitigation fee and an in lieu art fee as conditions of approval of plaintiff developer's project. The city contended the fees were constitutionally permissible monetary exactions reasonably related to legitimate public purposes, and not an unconstitutional taking of property.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, a city and the city council, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which invalidated the imposition of a development mitigation fee and an in lieu art fee as conditions of approval of plaintiff developer's project. Plaintiff successfully urged the trial court that requiring the fees as a condition of approval of the project constituted an unconstitutional taking of his land. The court reversed that holding and ruled that the land-use restrictions did not deprive plaintiff of all economically beneficial use of the land. The court found that the governmental purpose supporting a denial of the project was the loss of community recreational facilities which would be occasioned by the luxury townhouse development. The court found that the amount was not excessive or arbitrary, and that it was not compulsory. The court found that it was applicable only if plaintiff voluntarily chose to develop the property. The court found that the fee was not a special tax requiring approval by referendum, nor did it violate plaintiff's equal protection rights. The court also upheld the art fee levied in lieu of the placement of art on the project.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that part of the trial court's judgment which vacated the mitigation fee and awarded plaintiff developer attorney fees, and affirmed that part of the judgment which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate as to the in lieu art fee. The court remanded the case to the trial court to determine whether the lien filed by defendants, the city and the city council, should be reinstated to guarantee payment of the fees.

 

1016 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Rowe v. Superior Court


No. B070406


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


15 Cal. App. 4th 1711; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 625; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 554; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3846; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6583



May 25, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BC038955, Paul G. Flynn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. A peremptory writ shall issue directing the trial court to vacate its order of September 22, 1992, denying Rowe's motion to amend the complaint to allege claims for punitive damages and to conduct further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner plaintiffs sought a writ of mandate to issue requiring respondent trial court to allow amendment of its complaint pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 425.14 to allege a claim for punitive damages against tortfeasor church. Petitioner argued that § 425.14 is unconstitutional because that it violates California's constitutional guarantee of a jury trial and the establishment clause of the First Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner plaintiffs sought a writ of mandate to overturn respondent trial court's refusal to permit an amendment of their complaint against respondent church to allege a claim for punitive damages. Petitioner claimed that the requirement of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 425.14 that parties obtained court permission to plead for punitive damages was unconstitutional, in that it violated the establishment clause of the First Amendment and California's constitutional guarantee of a jury trial. The court applied the three-prong Lemon analysis and upheld the challenged statute because it had a valid secular purpose, did not have a primary effect of advancing religion or any particular religion, and did not foster excessive government entanglement in religion. The court found that § 425.14 was a reasonable accommodation of religion, which served the interests embodied in the free exercise clause and was not violative of the establishment clause. The court said that § 425.14 did not deprive litigants of a jury trial, but found that the trial judge erred when it applied the wrong standard to petitioner's evidentiary showing.


OUTCOME: The court determined that challenged legislative provision was violative of neither the California nor United States Constitutions, but that respondent trial court had applied an incorrect standard to petitioner plaintiff's evidentiary showing. The court issued a peremptory writ, which directed respondent trial court to vacate its order that denied petitioners' motion to amend the complaint to allege claims for punitive damages.

 

1017 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Souza


No. H009961


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE


15 Cal. App. 4th 1646; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 731; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 543; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3758; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6377



May 20, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 19, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4443.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 144867, Gregory H. Ward, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that denied his motion to suppress evidence. He summarily pled no contest to possession of methamphetamine for sale while armed with a firearm, driving with a suspended or revoked driver's license, resisting arrest, possession of a concealed firearm, and carrying a loaded weapon in a city.


OVERVIEW: After police stopped defendant, who was driving erratically, they saw an open tequila bottle behind the driver's seat. While retrieving the container, officer saw an envelope that contained a controlled substance. Defendant pled no contest to numerous charges, including possession of methamphetamine for sale while armed with a firearm, after a motion to suppress was denied. The court affirmed the judgment. Defendant claimed that the officer could not legally seize the open container because it was stored in an area not normally occupied by the driver or passengers, and Cal. Veh. Code § 23255 provided that an open container may be kept in an area of a vehicle not normally occupied by the driver or passengers. The court held rejected defendant's interpretation of the statute, finding that the legislature intended to make open containers inaccessible to drivers. The statute was not unconstitutionally vague because individuals of common intelligence could understand that § 23225 subjected individuals inside a vehicle without a trunk to liability if they stored open containers of alcohol within reach of the driver. Thus, seizure was proper and discovery of other evidence valid.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed. The court found that motion to suppress evidence of crimes was properly denied because the open container law provided that it was illegal to store an open container of an alcoholic beverage inside a vehicle without a trunk if the container was within reach of either the driver or his or her passengers. Thus, the officer had cause to seize the alcohol and ultimately discover the other incriminating evidence.

 

1018 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Aishman)


No. D018536


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


15 Cal. App. 4th 1593; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 444; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 541; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3828; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6594



May 19, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted and Opinion Vacated by order of the Court July 29, 1993 (S033133), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4151. Substituted Opinion August 19, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 855.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. ECR6713, Robert E. May, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the court to vacate its order of January 13, 1993, dismissing the section 422.75 allegations as unconstitutional, and to enter a new order consistent with this opinion. The stay issued on May 15, 1993, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought a writ commanding respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California) to set aside its order dismissing as unconstitutional Cal. Penal Code § 422.75(b) hate crime allegations against defendants, who were accused of assault with a deadly weapon and by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 245(a)(1), and battery with serious bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 243(d).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state sought a writ to set aside respondent trial court's dismissal, on constitutional grounds, of hate crime allegations under Cal. Penal Code § 422.75(b) against defendants, who were accused of assault with a deadly weapon and by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 245(a)(1), and battery with serious bodily injury, Cal. Penal Code § 243(d). The court granted the writ, holding that § 422.75(b) targeted discriminatory conduct, not speech. It was not vague under U.S. Const. amend. XIV when applied to an actor who selected his victim "because of" bias, because that phrase meant that bias was a substantial factor in the selection of the victim, and provided sufficient notice to defendants and guidelines for its application. It was not overbroad under U.S. Const. amend. I, because it applied only to felonious conduct, and required proof of a causal connection between the felony and the victim's status. It would not chill speech because expressions of racism would only be offered against the penal interest of the person holding such views when relevant to a criminal act. It constitutionally addressed motive, not thought.


OUTCOME: The court granted plaintiff state's petition for a writ, holding that the hate crimes sentence enhancement provision was constitutional under the First and Fourteenth Amendments, where it was directed to conduct, not speech, was not vague in that it gave sufficient notice of its applicability, was not overbroad because it applied only to felonious conduct, and did not chill speech where bias would only be relevant if there were a criminal act.

 

1019 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Cawdrey v. City of Redondo Beach


No. B056816


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


15 Cal. App. 4th 1212; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 179; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 516; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3533; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6051



May 12, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 1, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. YC004071, William C. Beverly, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, councilman and voter, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), denying their petition for a writ of mandamus and to enjoin defendants, city and city clerk, from enforcing a city charter term limit provision. The trial court ruled that the adoption of term limits was a municipal affair authorized by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a) and was not unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, council member and voter, challenged a city charter provision imposing term limits on elected city officials. The trial court denied plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandamus and to enjoin defendants, city and city clerk, from enforcing the limits. The court affirmed. The court found that the adoption of term limits was authorized by Cal. Const. art. XI, § 5(a), because it implicated a municipal affair, the election of municipal officers, and the issue of term limits for city officials was not of statewide concern. The court held that there was no conflict with the state statutory scheme governing local elections and that state law did not preempt the city charter provision. The court held that the provision did not violate the free speech or equal protection guarantees of the federal or state constitution, because it did not preclude plaintiff council member from seeking election to other offices, including that of mayor, and voters retained the right to vote for any qualified candidates. The court found that the interests of defendant city in incumbency reform outweighed any injury to incumbent officeholders and those who would vote for them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment denying the relief requested by plaintiffs, council member and voter, to enjoin defendants, city and city clerk, from enforcing limits on terms for city officials. The court held that the city charter term limit provision was authorized under the state constitution, and that it was not preempted by state law governing local elections.

Lucero v. Mun. Court


No. B050440


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


15 Cal. App. 4th 784; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 143; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 495; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3397; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5829



May 7, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 12, 1993 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4324. Review Denied as moot February 9, 1994, Reported at: 1994 Cal. LEXIS 642.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C715969, Jack M. Newman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The award of attorney fees and costs is affirmed. Respondents to recover their costs of appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant City of Long Beach appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which awarded fees and costs to respondents, individuals and corporation affiliated with a movie theater, pursuant to the Civil Rights Attorney's Fee Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, as prevailing parties in an action to restrain a criminal prosecution brought by the city in violation of their constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The trial court ordered defendant City of Long Beach to pay respondents, individuals and corporation affiliated with a movie theater, attorney fees pursuant to the Civil Rights Attorney's Fee Awards Act of 1976, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1988, in connection with their successful constitutional challenge of a zoning ordinance. On appeal, defendant argued that the award was improper as a matter of law. The court held that respondents were entitled to attorney fees as the prevailing parties. The doctrine of prosecutorial immunity did not apply because respondents had sought prospective relief and the liability of the municipality, not that of the prosecutor, was at issue. The fact that only the People, real party in interest, and appellant, were named in the pleadings was of no consequence because the course of proceedings revealed defendant as the real party in interest and it had adequate notice of the suit and an opportunity to defend. The court held that an award of § 1988 fees in a writ proceeding arising from an attempted criminal prosecution was consistent with Congress' intent to authorize such awards to a prevailing party in any proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of attorney fees and costs to respondents, individuals and corporation affiliated with a movie theater, pursuant to the Civil Rights Attorney's Fee Awards Act of 1976 because respondents were the prevailing parties and defendant City of Long Beach was not shielded from liability under the doctrine of prosecutorial immunity.

California Radioactive Materials Management Forum v. Department of Health Services


No. C013930


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE


15 Cal. App. 4th 841; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 357; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 500; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3443; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5904



May 7, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING; application for writ of mandate.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue requiring the respondents to set aside their order for further administrative hearings and to proceed with consideration of the application of US Ecology, Inc., for a low-level radioactive waste disposal license, without regard to or further consideration of the void agreement with the Senate Rules Committee. The alternative writ having served its purpose is discharged. The stay previously issued is vacated with the finality of this decision.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a proposed licensee, individuals and groups, requested writs of mandate and prohibition to prohibit respondents, Department of Health Services, its director and the secretary of the Health and Welfare Agency, from conducting an administrative hearing on petitioner proposed licensee's application for a low-level radioactive waste disposal license, based on an agreement reached with the real party in interest, Senate Rules Committee.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Department of Health Services (department) chose petitioner proposed licensee to construct a low-level radioactive waste disposal facility and scheduled hearings on the license application. Several individuals and groups demanded an Administrative Procedure Act (APA), Cal. Gov't Code §§ 11500-11517, type of hearing. Before respondent department could render a decision, the real party in interest, Senate Rules Committee, intervened. The latter held confirmation hearings on the appointments of respondent director and secretary, both of whom opined that APA-type hearings were not required. They were informed that their confirmations by the full Senate would have been difficult, so they eventually agreed to the hearings and their nominations were approved by the Senate. The court held that the real party in interest, Senate Rules Committee, interfered in the administration of the law, that its actions were unconstitutional, and that the purported agreement with respondent director and secretary was void. The law did not require formal adjudicatory hearings. The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate ordering respondents to proceed with the license application.


OUTCOME: The court held in favor of petitioners, proposed licensee, individuals and groups, and issued a writ of mandate to respondents, Department of Health Services, its director, and the secretary of the Health and Welfare Agency, setting aside the order for an administrative hearing and ordering them to proceed with petitioner proposed licensee's application disregarding the void agreement reached with real party in interest, Senate Rules Committee.

 

1022 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Savedra


No. B063631


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN.


15 Cal. App. 4th 738; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 115; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 487; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3350; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5759



May 6, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 11, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4221.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. PA006101, Jacqueline L. Weisberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged his conviction by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for possessing a deadly weapon while lawfully confined in jail in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4574(a) after a sharpened nail was found in appellant's pocket during a routine contraband search.


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate was subjected to a routine contraband search and a sharpened nail was found in his pocket. Appellant was convicted of possessing a deadly weapon while lawfully confined in jail under Cal. Penal Code § 4574(a). On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the trial court's refusal to give Cal. Jury Instructions - Civil No. 12.42, which enumerated factors to consider in determining whether an object was a weapon, was not error because No. 12.42 did not construe § 4574 (a). At trial, the jury requested a definition of the word "likely" in the phrase "likely to cause great bodily harm" and the court's response that it meant "has the potential for use as a deadly weapon" was not error. The court found that it was not prejudicial to admit the nail in an evidence bag that read, "3 inch nail with toilet paper handle," because a deputy testified that he wrote the words on the bag and hence they did not amount to an unsupported characterization by an anonymous declarant. The court vacated appellant's sentence imposed under Cal. Penal Code § 667.5(b), because the record did not reflect the trial court's reasoning behind the sentence and remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant inmate's conviction for possessing a weapon while lawfully confined in jail because the trial court did not err in refusing to give a jury instruction which enumerated factors to consider in determining whether an object was a weapon. The court vacated appellant's sentence and remanded to the trial court for resentencing because the record did not reflect the reasoning behind the sentence.

 

1023 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Diesel Electric Sales & Service, Inc. v. Marco Marine San Diego, Inc.


No. D014843


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


16 Cal. App. 4th 202; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 62; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 587; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4150; 93 Daily Journal DAR 6995; 1995-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P71,131



May 5, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published June 3, 1993. Review Denied July 22 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3910.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 541017, Benjamin W. Hamrick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. Diesel to recover its costs of this appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff distributor appealed the nonsuit judgment entered by the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in favor of defendant distributor on plaintiff's claims that defendant violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17045, a provision of California's Unfair Practices Act.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff distributor appealed the nonsuit judgment in favor of defendant distributor on plaintiff's claims that defendant received secret unearned discounts in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17045, part of California's Unfair Practices Act. The court reversed, holding that plaintiff's evidence, when viewed in a light most favorable to it, established a prima facie violation of § 17045 where defendant utilized its affiliations with its parent company and other subsidiaries to induce the manufacturer supplying both parties to sell its products to defendant at the maximum discount off the list price, regardless of order size. Plaintiff, meanwhile, was misled as to defendant's arrangement, had to earn higher volume discounts on a per order basis, and suffered losses. Further, defendant could not use its affiliations with an original equipment manufacturer as a pretext to procure advantageous pricing over fellow distributors where defendant was not using the products for manufacturing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the nonsuit judgment because plaintiff's evidence, when viewed in a light most favorable to it, established that defendant received a secret allowance of an unearned discount, injured plaintiff as a competitor, and destroyed competition. Further, defendant's affiliations alone did not render it an original equipment manufacturer.

 

1024 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davis


No. D016246


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


20 Cal. App. 4th 657; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 96; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 483; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3359; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5777



May 4, 1993, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted August 25, 1993 (S033327), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 4487.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 15 Cal. App. 4th 690.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR122543, Michael D. Wellington, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed except for the conviction of fetal murder, which is reversed. The fetal murder court is remanded to the superior court for retrial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment entered in the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), where he was convicted of murder of a fetus during the course of a robbery, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187(a), 290.2(a)(17) and of other charges. The court sentenced him to life without the possibility of parole plus five years. Defendant contended that the trial court prejudicially erred when it instructed the jury on fetal viability.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed the trial court's judgment that included a conviction for the murder of a fetus during the course of a robbery. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment except for the conviction of fetal murder, which was reversed and remanded for retrial. The court found that the trial court's jury instruction that defined fetal viability under then existing law was prejudicially erroneous. The court also held that the criminalization of feticide without regard to the level of fetal development did not violate a mother's privacy interest in terminating her pregnancy, equal protection or due process; therefore, no reason existed not to apply Cal. Penal Code § 187(a), in accordance with its plain meaning. Finally the court held that although not retroactive in application, the killing of a fetus with malice aforethought, which killing did not fall under any of the exceptions listed in § 187(b), was murder; therefore, in order for a fetus to be a subject of the murder statute, it need only be shown that it had progressed beyond the embryonic stage of seven to eight weeks. The court rejected the requirement that a fetus be viable in order to be the subject of murder §187.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court's instructions to the jury that defined fetal viability under the law as it then existed were prejudicially erroneous. The court held that it was therefore required to reverse the judgment of murder and remand for a new trial on that count. The court modified the elements of fetal murder, and held its new interpretation should not be applied to cases that arose before the publication of the court's opinion.

Kahn v. Department of Motor Vehicles


No. B064070


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


16 Cal. App. 4th 159; 20 Cal. Rptr. 2d 6; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 597; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4265; 93 Daily Journal DAR 7083



May 3, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Ordered Published May 5, 1993. Review Denied July 22, 1993, Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 3902.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. BS009161, Ronald M. Sohigian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner driver sought review of a judgment rendered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that denied her petition for a writ of mandate by which she sought to compel respondent Department of Motor Vehicles to return her personalized license plates.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner driver had a personalized license plate that bore the court reporting symbols "TP U BG," which translated into words, meant "if you can." Officials realized that petitioner's license plate read as "F." "U." "CK" or "K" in stenographic shorthand for certain letters. Respondent Department of Motor Vehicles asked petitioner to surrender her personalized license plates. Petitioner sought review from a judgment that denied her petition for a writ of mandate by which she sought to compel respondent to return her personalized license plates. Petitioner asserted there was insufficient evidence to support the cancellation of her license plate as one carrying "connotations offensive to good taste and decency" as stated in Cal. Veh. Code § 5505. The court ruled that there was substantial evidence to show that petitioner's personalized plate did not meet the standard of § 5505. Petitioner argued that it is not enough that the translation of the court reporting symbols as the four-letter epithet be offensive that there must also have been evidence that "TP U BG" was in itself offensive. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied petitioner driver 's writ of mandate by which she sought to compel Respondent Department of Motor Vehicles to return her personalized license plates. The translated letters on petitioner's license plate were offensive to some and there was substantial evidence to affirm the judgment.

Stolz v. Bank of America


No. C012927


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE


15 Cal. App. 4th 217; 19 Cal. Rptr. 2d 19; 1993 Cal. App. LEXIS 455; 93 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3106; 93 Daily Journal DAR 5293



April 27, 1993, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* See footnote 2, post, page 219.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. CV519753, Ronald B. Robie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant litigant sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which dismissed his action against appellee bank because he failed to furnish security as required by the vexatious litigant statutes contained in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 391 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant litigant had been involved in numerous lawsuits. Appellee bank sought to require security from appellant as indicated by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 391. Appellant contended that he did not meet the definition of a vexatious litigant. The court determined that the only case relied on by appellee to which appellant had registered a challenge did meet the criteria set forth in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 391(b)(1), which specified litigation that was commenced, prosecuted or maintained within seven years. The court found that appellant had failed to furnish security as required by the statute. Accordingly, the court affirmed the prior dismissal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of dismissal because it determined that appellant had failed to provide security for continued litigation as required of a vexatious litigant by the vexatious litigant statutes.
