Resolution Trust Corp. v. Winslow


No. H006506


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 1799; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 510; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1190; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8356; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13646; 20 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. 2d (Callaghan) 1062



October 5, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 576533, Mark E. Thomas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The money judgment for Winslow is reversed and remanded. The trial court is ordered to enter judgment on Winslow's cross-complaint for RTC. The specific performance decree is reversed. The parties shall bear their own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Resolution Trust Authority sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) ordering plaintiff to pay defendant $ 415,000 on defendant's cross-complaint for damages resulting from negligent misrepresentations made with respect to the parties' security agreement. Defendant challenged the trial court's decision finding defendant liable for breach of contract and ordering specific performance.


OVERVIEW: In plaintiff Resolution Trust Authority's action for breach of contract, defendant challenged the trial court's judgment ordering defendant to specifically perform by conveying stock as set forth in the parties' security agreement. On defendant's cross-complaint for detrimental reliance, plaintiff contested the trial court's judgment ordering plaintiff to pay defendant $ 415,000 for negligent misrepresentation. The parties' written contract provided that plaintiff would invest money in defendant's business in exchange for an assignment of stock when certain conditions precedent were met. On appeal, the court held that plaintiff was not liable for making misrepresentations because the federal common law D'Oench, Duhme doctrine, codified at 12 U.S.C.S. 1823(e), preempted state law and barred introduction of the parties' unwritten understandings in determining the parties' intent in the written contract. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment in favor of defendant. Because specific performance was not a statutory remedy for breach of a security agreement, the court also reversed the decision in favor of plaintiff. The court reversed as to both the appeal and the cross-appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision in favor of defendant on defendant's cross-complaint for negligent misrepresentation because federal law barred introduction of parole evidence regarding breach of the parties' written contract. The court reversed the trial court's decision in favor of plaintiff Resolution Trust Authority because specific performance was not a statutorily permitted remedy for breach of a security agreement.

 

1094 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Fearn v. Zolin


No. F016733


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 1756; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 314; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1183; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8303; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13564



October 2, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 437650-5, Robert Z. Mardikian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings, if appropriate, and for reconsideration and disposition not inconsistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state agency appealed an order of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which granted respondent a writ of mandate to set aside an order of petitioner that suspended respondent's driving privileges for second offenders under Cal. Veh. Code § 13353.2(a), and 13353.3(b)(2). Respondent claimed there was a conflict between the administrative per se suspension and probation statutes allowing him a restricted license.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was arrested for driving under the influence, and petitioner state agency subsequently suspended respondent's driving privileges for second offenders under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 13353.2(a), and 13353.3(b)(2). Respondent pleaded guilty and the trial court placed him on probation and restricted his driver's license to travel from and to work under Cal. Veh. Code §§ 23165, 23166(b). Petitioner refused to grant respondent a restricted license. At a hearing on petitioner's suspension order, respondent argued that there was a conflict between the administrative per se suspension law and the probation statutes which provided for license restriction rather than suspension. Petitioner upheld its order and respondent filed for a writ of mandate with the trial court to vacate the order, which the trial court granted. Petitioner appealed the ruling, and the court reversed the order and remanded the case. The court held that the laws did not conflict. The legislature intended two processes, one criminal and one administrative, to address drunk driving concerns and the statutes did not violate due process rights, because the criminal and civil dispositions ran concurrently.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting respondent's writ of mandate to set aside an order of petitioner state agency, which suspended respondent's driving privileges for second offenders, because the legislature provided for two independent proceedings to address drunk driving. The criminal punishment and administrative sanctions imposed on respondent served different purposes and did not violate due process rights. The case was remanded.

People v. Patten


No. F015537


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 1718; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 284; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1170; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8248; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13513



October 1, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II through VII.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied January 13, 1993, Reported at 1993 Cal LEXIS 74.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 29388, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of jury convictions from the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) for six counts of forcible rape in violation of Cal. Pen. Code § 361(2) and two counts of oral copulation by force in violation of Cal. Pen. Code § 288a(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged jury convictions for six counts of forcible rape under Cal. Pen. Code § 361(2) and two counts of oral copulation by force under Cal. Pen. Code § 288a(c). Defendant kidnapped a woman from her home, beating and raping her repeatedly for a period of several hours before she was able to escape. On appeal, the court held that, under Cal. Pen. Code § 868.5, the trial court did not err in permitting the victim's sister to sit in the courtroom as a support person while the victim testified. There was no evidence suggesting that the sister's presence unduly influenced or prejudiced the jury in reaching its verdicts. The fact that the sister testified prior to acting as a support person also diminished any possibility of prejudice. Other than mentioning in closing argument that the jury had observed "two crying women," defense counsel failed to make a record specifying why defendant's due process right to a fair trial was abridged by the support person's presence. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's multiple convictions for rape, holding that defendant's due process right to a fair trial was not impaired by the presence of a support witness in the courtroom during the victim's testimony, as permitted by law. Defense counsel failed to create an adequate record to show that his client was prejudiced by the support witness's attendance.

People v. Hammond


No. E008739


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO.


9 Cal. App. 4th 1523; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 205; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1164; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8192; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13408



September 30, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Modified October 16, 1992. Review Denied December 31, 1992 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6508.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR 34327, James T. Warren, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The matter is remanded for resentencing on the prior felony conviction under section 667.5, subdivision (b) in a manner consistent with the views stated in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County, California, convicting him of selling and transporting marijuana, second-degree murder, unlawful driving or taking of a vehicle, and attempting to evade a pursuing police vehicle.


OVERVIEW: During a pursuit that extended over several miles, defendant ran five stop signs and two red lights with a car that was stolen; he also drove on the curb to pass cars and to cut off a tractor-trailer rig. As a result, defendant was involved in an automobile accident that killed another individual. The court admitted evidence of defendant's outstanding arrest warrant for a parole violation. Defendant pleaded guilty to selling and transporting marijuana, and the jury convicted him of second-degree murder, unlawful driving or taking of a vehicle, and attempting to evade a pursuing police vehicle. The trial court rejected defendant's contention that no evidence showed he was intoxicated during the pursuit, and that intoxication was an essential element for a conviction of second-degree murder. The court determined that fleeing from a police in a high speed chase supported the conclusion that, even without being intoxicated, he had implied malice, which supported the conviction. Although the jury instruction was erroneously phrased in unqualified terms, the jury would have reached the same decision because of defendant's dangerous acts. His convictions were affirmed.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction of second-degree murder, among others, was affirmed because his fleeing from a police high speed chase showed that he had implied malice to support his conviction for second-degree murder.

Hines v. Superior Court


No. D016932


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


20 Cal. App. 4th 1099; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 216; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1166; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8187; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13425



September 30, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted December 31, 1992 (S029613), Reported at: 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6523.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 9 Cal. App. 4th 1500 and 15 Cal. App. 4th 1029.)] Dismissed January 13, 1994 as moot.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 125452, Laura P. Hammes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Petition granted. The defense cannot be required to disclose penalty trial witnesses and evidence to the prosecution pursuant to section 1054.3 before a guilty verdict and a finding of special circumstance(s) have been returned.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), holding the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1053 et seq., authorizing prosecutorial discovery to apply to the penalty phase of a capital case before there was a guilty verdict along with a finding of special circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with one count of murder with three special circumstances, making him eligible for the death penalty. The trial court granted discovery motions of the parties. Pursuant to the discovery provisions of the Cal. Penal Code § 1053 et seq., the trial court ruled that the provisions applied to the penalty phase as well as the guilt phase of a capital case. Defendant now challenged the order of the court requiring him to disclose, before trial commenced, the witnesses it intended to call during the penalty phase. On appeal, the court held that the statute was ambiguous, taking to consideration the context of a bifurcated capital case, in which the issues of guilt and punishment were always tried in separate proceedings. The court held that with respect to a capital case, the statute did not mandate the defense disclosure of evidence it intended to present at the penalty trial until a guilty verdict and finding of special circumstances have been returned in the guilt trial. It reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order requiring defendant to disclose penalty trial witnesses to the prosecution before a guilt verdict. It held that the provisions of the statute did not apply to capital cases, where issues and punishment were always tried in separate proceedings.

Griset v. Fair Political Practices Com.


No. G011724.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


25 Cal. App. 4th 1527; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 249; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1171; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8231; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13529



September 30, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted January 21, 1993 (S029701), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 371.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 9 Cal. App. 4th 1559 and 15 Cal. App. 4th 1060 and 20 Cal. App. 4th 1114.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 63-86-54, Robert A. Knox, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Parties to bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate and his campaign committees sought review of a decision from the Superior Court, Orange County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus, which challenged punitive measures undertaken against them by respondent Fair Political Practices Commission. Respondent found appellants violated Cal. Gov't Code § 84305 by sending out anonymous campaign literature.


OVERVIEW: Appellant candidate and his campaign committees circulated anonymous political mailings. Respondent political commission brought punitive measures against appellants. An administrative law judge determined that appellants' actions violated Cal. Gov't Code § 84305, which required the candidate or the committee sending out such mailings to identify themselves. Appellants contended the statute was unconstitutional and sought a writ of mandamus, declaratory relief, and injunctive relief. Their motions for a peremptory writ and for summary judgment were denied. On appeal, the lower court's decision was affirmed. The appellate court held that the state had a compelling interest in an informed electorate, in assuring that the voters are provided with information which will aid them in evaluating the candidates and that § 84305, as applied to appellants, promoted the state's compelling interest in an informed electorate. The appellate court applied a limited construction of the statute to ensure narrow application of the disclosure rule in order to avoid a declaration of unconstitutionality based on overbreadth.


OUTCOME: The denial of appellant candidate and his campaign committees' writ seeking review of respondent political commission's assessment of punitive measures against them was affirmed. The appellate court held the applicable provision of the California Government Code was constitutional as applied to appellants and that its potential overbreadth was cured by the interpretation that it applied only to candidates and candidate controlled committees.

In re Anita G.


Nos. G011386, G011850


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


15 Cal. App. 4th 1044; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 265; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1179; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 8236; 92 Daily Journal DAR 13542



September 30, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted December 17, 1992 (S029642), Reported at: 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6263.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 9 Cal. App. 4th 1543.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-417286, Gary G. Bischoff, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: Thus, we reverse the order terminating Ana's parental rights and remand the matter for a new hearing under section 366.22, directing the trial court to weigh the evidence of substantial risk of detriment under the clear and convincing standard. Ana's writ petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a temporary conservatee, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which terminated her parental rights, claiming the court erred by conducting the termination hearing without the presence of her public guardian, resulting in a denial of her right to the effective assistance of counsel, and denial of due process because the termination of parental rights was based on a preponderance of the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mother, who had been placed under a temporary conservatorship, appealed the order of the lower court, which terminated her parental rights. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the parent was denied due process because Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 unconstitutionally authorized the trial court to terminate her parental rights based on findings made by a preponderance of the evidence and found this lesser burden of proof constitutionally insufficient. The court held that the question of the adoptability of the child should not be permitted to supplant or influence the independent finding concerning parental unfitness. The court held that it was error to fail to appoint a guardian ad litem for the mother, but found the error to be harmless. The trial court was informed that the parent had been placed under a temporary conservatorship and a permanent appointment was pending; this was sufficient to require the appointment of a guardian ad litem.


OUTCOME: The order terminating defendant temporary conservatee's parental rights was reversed and remanded for a new hearing directing the trial court to weigh the evidence of substantial risk of detriment under the clear and convincing standard because the severance of the parent-child relationship on less than clear and convincing evidence was constitutionally insufficient. The failure to appoint a guardian was error, but harmless.

In re David F.


No. G011896


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 17, 1992, and Reporter of Decisions is directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6297.
Kennard, J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-42-24-32, Gary L. Vincent, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: An appropriate service plan has not been in place since the six-month review hearing; consequently, the judgment is reversed with directions to proceed anew from the time of the erroneous ruling at that hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which terminated his parental rights, contending that the reunification plan of respondent social services agency was not reasonable and could not be used to terminate his parental rights and that the finding of detriment to the child based upon a preponderance of the evidence was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father sought review of the order that terminated his parental rights, contending that the reunification plan of respondent social services agency was not reasonable and that the finding of detriment to the child based upon a preponderance of the evidence was unconstitutional. The court reversed the order that terminated appellant's parental rights finding that the reunification plan of respondent, which was approved while appellant was incarcerated, was unreasonable because it did not take into account his incarceration and was irrelevant to his circumstances. The court concluded that such a plan could not later be used as a basis to terminate appellant's parental rights and directed respondent to proceed with an appropriate plan. The court determined that appellant's parental right to raise his child was a fundamental interest that could only be terminated by a showing of clear and convincing evidence and any lesser burden of proof was unconstitutional. The court found that in the proceeding to terminate parental rights the child's best interests were not the only consideration, but had to be balanced against the fundamental interests of appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that terminated the parental rights of appellant father with directions to proceed with an appropriate reunification plan, finding that the plan approved by respondent social services agency was unreasonable and should not have been the basis of termination of parental rights. The court determined that the finding of detriment was unconstitutionally determined by a preponderance of the evidence.

Clare v. State Bd. of Accountancy


No. D014811


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


10 Cal. App. 4th 294; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 481; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1225; 92 Daily Journal DAR 14157



September 22, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 14, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 631974, Anthony C. Joseph, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant accountant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered a judgment denying his petition for a writ of mandate challenging the suspension of his accountant's license by respondent state board. He alleged that respondent erred by suspending his state license under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5100(g) solely because he had been earlier suspended by the Federal Home Loan Bank Board.


OVERVIEW: Appellant accountant was suspended by the Federal Home Loan Bank Board (FHLBB) because of improprieties perceived in his audit performance of a savings and loan. Respondent state board of accountancy later filed an accusation seeking to revoke appellant's license to practice accountancy in the state pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5100. An administrative law judge made specific findings that the FHLBB suspension was substantially related to the qualifications, functions, or duties of the accounting profession, and respondent revoked appellant's license. The trial court refused to issue a writ of mandate to set the decision. The court affirmed the decision. The court concluded a reasonable interpretation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 5100(g) included a requirement that a licensee's prior suspension be substantially related to conduct pertaining to qualifications, functions, or duties as an accountant to justify discipline under § 5100. It further concluded substantial evidence supported the trial court's and respondent's findings that appellant's conduct underlying the FHLBB suspension was substantially related to those qualifications, functions, or duties.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court properly denied appellant accountant's petition for a writ of mandate because there was substantial evidence to support respondent state board's judgment, and there was an implicit requirement that a licensee's prior suspension be substantially related to conduct pertaining to qualification, functions, and duties as an accountant to justify discipline.

In re Elizabeth T.


No. C010785


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 636; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 10; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1109; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7912; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12763



September 15, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied December 3, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6077.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 202801, 202802, 202803 and 202804, Peter Dixon, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that admitted testimony of his minor children over objection pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code § 1293, ordered the minors' placed in mother's home, and issued a no-contact order against father.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father challenged the judgment of the trial court that admitted testimony of his minor children over objection pursuant to Cal. Evid. Code § 1293, ordered the minors' placed in mother's home, and issued a no-contact order against father. Appellant facially challenged the constitutionality of § 1293 contending that admitting the preliminary hearing testimony of the minors unconstitutionally deprived him of his right to confront witnesses as guaranteed by due process. The court relied on controlling precedent which held that a state's interest in protecting child victims could outweigh the right of confrontation and maintained that the legislature could properly find it unnecessary to require a showing of unavailability before a juvenile could admit preliminary hearing testimony of a minor. The court also held that the presumption underlying § 1293 was that testimony and cross-examination from the former proceeding as recorded in the transcript provided a sufficient means for the court to assess credibility of nontestifying witnesses, and that the presumption was constitutionally valid. Accordingly the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The disposition order was affirmed. The court held that a state's interest in protecting child victims could outweigh the right of confrontation, therefore the court rejected appellant's constitutional challenge to the governing statute under which minors' testimony from a preliminary hearing was admitted in juvenile court.

 

1103 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Heather B.


No. C010299


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 535; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 891; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1104; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7833; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12687



September 14, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied November 24, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5828.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 15380, William R. Lund, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the juvenile court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Shasta County (California), which terminated his parental rights and placed his minor daughter for adoption pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26. Appellant contended that his constitutional rights had been violated by the termination of his parental rights under an improper burden of proof.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court found that the use of a preponderance of evidence standard at the review hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 was constitutionally permissible, given that it already had been shown, by clear and convincing evidence, that the child had to be removed from appellant father's physical custody pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 361(b). The court noted that it was only on the narrow question of whether the child would have been subjected to a substantial risk of harm in a return to a home which already had been found to be unfit that the legislature had chosen a preponderance of the evidence standard. The court then rejected appellant's assertion that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.21(f) constituted an unconstitutional presumption of parental unfitness, finding that the statute did not establish a presumption affecting appellant's burden of proof, but rather a rebuttable presumption to be reached by respondent agency with respect to parental unfitness. The court affirmed, concluding that substantial evidence supported the order that terminated appellant's parental rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that terminated appellant father's parental rights and placed the dependent child for adoption where the preponderance of the evidence at the review hearing established that a return to the custody of appellant, who already had been shown to be unfit by clear and convincing evidence at the initial disposition hearing, would have created a substantial risk to the child's physical or emotional well-being.

Traverso v. People ex rel. Dep't of Transportation


No. A055785


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


20 Cal. App. 4th 606; 12 Cal. Rptr. 2d 1; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1099; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7771; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12612



September 10, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing October 13, 1993, Reported at: 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1215.
Review Granted December 17, 1992 (S029371), Reported at: 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6258.
[Reprinted as modified to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cummulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cites 9 Cal. App. 4th 477 and 15 Cal. App. 4th 388.)] (See 6 cal. 4th 1152)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sonoma County, No. 160324, Laurence K. Sawyer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in favor of Caltrans is reversed. The case is remanded with instructions to enter judgment in favor of Traverso consistent with this opinion. Costs to Traverso.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff billboard owner sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Sonoma County (California), dismissing on summary judgment plaintiff's action seeking a declaration that the first paragraph of section 5463 of the Outdoor Advertising Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 5200 et seq., unconstitutionally deprived plaintiff of his due process rights.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff billboard owner challenged the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendant department of transportation, holding that plaintiff's due process rights had not been violated. After plaintiff's billboard either blew down or was vandalized, defendant revoked plaintiff's nonconforming use permit without a hearing. Plaintiff argued that the first paragraph of section 5463 of the Outdoor Advertising Act (Act), Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 5200 et seq., unconstitutionally deprived him of a significant property interest without notice and opportunity to be heard. On appeal, the court agreed. Section 5463 of the Act permitted defendant to revoke without a hearing plaintiff's viable nonconforming use permit for failure to comply with the Act's requirements. Because no hearing record existed, the court could not ascertain whether or not the billboard fell down due to plaintiff's failure to conform to the Act. Because the Act provided plaintiff with no meaningful due process either before or after the revocation of his permit, the first paragraph of section 5463 of the Act was unconstitutional. The court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to defendant department of transportation and declared a portion of an outdoor advertising statute unconstitutional because the statute deprived plaintiff billboard owner of his property without giving plaintiff notice or a meaningful opportunity to be heard, thereby vitiating plaintiff's due process rights.

Southern Pacific Pipe Lines, Inc. v. Board of Supervisors

COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT

In re Littlefield


No. B061445


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE.


9 Cal. App. 4th 329; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 703; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1077; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7689; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12451



September 3, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified October 1, 1992.
Review Granted November 19, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5767. ( See 5 Cal. 4th 122).


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court of the Glendale Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 91M02993, James E. Rogan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a public defender, filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus after the Municipal Court of the Glendale Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California) found him in contempt of court for refusing to comply with a reciprocal discovery order made pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1054.3 in a driving under the influence case.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a public defender, was held in contempt of court by the court below for refusing to comply with a reciprocal discovery order made pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1054.3 in a driving under the influence case. Petitioner refused to comply with an order that directed him to comply with the prosecution's request for discovery of statements made by a specified defense witness. Petitioner refused to comply with the order upon the argument that a local court rule provided that the defense was entitled to 15 days' formal notice of any prosecution request for a formal discovery order. The court below found petitioner in contempt after it found that Cal. Penal Code § 1054.5 prevailed over the local rule and after it found that petitioner's request for 15 days' notice was untimely. The court denied a petition for writ of habeas corpus filed that was subsequently filed by petitioner because Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1005(b) and the local rule at issue provided that their time limits could be shortened by a court. Petitioner's good faith belief that the reciprocal discovery law was unconstitutional did not protect his deliberate violation of the lower court's order.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's application for a writ of habeas corpus, which was filed after the court below found him in contempt of court for refusing to comply with a reciprocal discovery order, was denied, even though petitioner had a good faith belief that the discovery order was unlawful, because petitioner should have petitioned the court for a writ of mandate instead of willfully violating the order at his own peril.

People v. Benites


No. F015311


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 309; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 512; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1072; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7633; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12319



September 2, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tuolumne County, Nos. 28581 and 28934, Gerard Kettmenn, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Santa Clara Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Tuolumne County (California), which convicted him of receiving stolen property, being an ex-felon in possession of firearms, possession of burglary tools, and possession of narcotics. Appellant argued that the trial court improperly denied his motion to suppress the items at issue, which were discovered during an inventory search of his van following impoundment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by the court below of several counts of receiving stolen property, being an ex-felon in possession of firearms, possession of burglary tools, and possession of narcotics. The property was discovered after a routine traffic stop which revealed that appellant and his passenger both had suspended licenses. The officer decided to impound the van and conduct an inventory search because the van was parked on an isolated stretch of road, because there was the possibility that appellant would simply drive off once he left, because it was late at night, and because the van could be vandalized if left on the highway. The inventory search revealed property that led to appellant's conviction. The trial court's judgment was affirmed on appeal. The court found that the traffic stop, decision to impound, and subsequent inventory search were reasonable under the circumstances, even though the sheriff's department lacked a written policy on impoundment, because the deputies were given clear parameters under which to exercise their discretion pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 22651(p). Therefore, the trial court properly denied appellant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: Appellant's conviction for receiving stolen property, being an ex-felon in possession of firearms, possession of burglary tools, and possession of narcotics was affirmed. The trial court was found to have properly denied appellant's motion to suppress the items at issue, which were discovered during an inventory search of his van following impoundment, because the traffic stop and decision to impound were reasonable under the circumstances.

 

1108 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Higgins


No. C010490


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 294; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 694; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1076; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7614; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12284



September 2, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II, III, IV.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. C-42769, Carl F. Bryan II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Nevada County (California), which convicted him of one count of continuous sexual abuse of a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288.5. Appellant argued on appeal that § 288.5 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of one count of continuous sexual abuse of a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288.5. Appellant argued on appeal that § 288.5 unconstitutionally deprived an accused of due process by failing to give notice of the specific acts of molestation with which he is charged and deprives the criminal defendant of the right to a unanimous jury verdict by permitting a conviction without unanimity on which acts of molestation occurred. Appellant's conviction was affirmed on appeal after the court determined that § 288.5 was constitutional. By criminalizing a course of conduct, § 288.5 was intended to make proof of specific acts unnecessary. The fact that the jurors select different acts to satisfy the numerical threshold for a course of conduct did not render the statute unconstitutional. Rather, it assured that the jury must agree that there was a course of conduct. Moreover, it prevented convictions based on a single act or a series of acts upon which the jury did not agree. The court found that the information, coupled with the victim's testimony at the preliminary hearing, had more than adequately apprised appellant of the nature of the charges against him.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court below, which convicted appellant of one count of continuous sexual abuse of a child, was affirmed on appeal. Contrary to appellant's contention, the court found that the California statute, which categorized as a specific crime the continuous sexual abuse of a child under 14 years of age, did not unconstitutionally deprive an accused of due process.

 

1109 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Superior Court (Sturm)


No. G012303, No. G012309


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


9 Cal. App. 4th 172; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 652; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1063; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7698; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12393



August 31, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 16, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. C-82847 and C-57585, David O. Carter and Kathleen E. O'Leary, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue as prayed in G012303.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated writ proceedings involving unrelated capital cases, one petitioner sought writ of mandamus to direct respondent, the Superior Court of Orange County, (California), to vacate its order granting the prosecution's motion to compel the identification of witnesses to be called in the penalty phases under the reciprocal discovery provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1054 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The court consolidated review in unrelated capital cases, in which one petitioner' requested a writ of mandamus in connection with the application of the reciprocal discovery provision under Cal. Penal Code § 1054 et seq., to the penalty phase of their capital cases. The court denied the petition, concluding the reciprocal discovery provisions required defense disclosure of penalty phase evidence and such requirement was not unconstitutional. The court explained the statutory scheme requiring both defense and prosecution disclosure did not entitle the prosecution to any form of discovery not available to the defense. The court further determined the word "trial" as used in the statute applied to the penalty phase of a trial. The court reasoned during the penalty phase, the jury addressed itself to ultimate issues and had to sift through facts in evaluating the substance of the defendant's record, history, mental condition, and circumstances. The court noted under such circumstance, a defendant was not entitled to an opportunity to present false or misleading information. The court found the statute did not violate due process or the privilege against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's request for writ of mandamus against respondent state's application of reciprocal discovery provisions during the penalty phase of capital cases because such provisions applied to the penalty phase where petitioner was not entitled to present misleading or untruthful information and reciprocal discovery did not violate due process or privilege against self-incrimination.

People v. Smith


No. F014729


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


9 Cal. App. 4th 196; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 645; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1065; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7554; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12178



August 31, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of the Statement of the Case, the Statement of Facts, and the Discussion, parts I, II.A., II.B., III.C., III.D., and IV.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 42498, Clarence Westra, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments are reversed as to the section 12022, subdivision (c) allegations, and the sentences imposed thereon are vacated. Retrial of defendants with respect to these enhancements is barred.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions in the Superior Court of Kern County, No. 42498 (California), which found them guilty of charges arising out of a conspiracy involving the possession for sale and transportation of large amounts of methamphetamine.


OVERVIEW: Defendants appealed their convictions on an array of charges, which arose out of a conspiracy involving the possession for sale and transportation of large amounts of methamphetamine. Defendants claimed that that the trial court erred in instructing the jury regarding the imposition of enhancements under Cal. Penal Code § 12022(c). The court reversed the trial court judgments as to the § 12022(c) allegations, and the sentences imposed thereon were vacated. The court held that the trial court erred because it did not instruct the jury that a defendant must be "personally armed" for purposes of § 12022(c) and the instruction actually given improperly permitted a jury finding of "true" based solely on vicarious liability. The court vacated the remaining portions of the judgments, which imposed sentence upon each defendant, and the matters were each remanded for resentencing. In all other respects, the judgments were affirmed. The court rejected defendants' claim that the reasonable doubt jury instruction, Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 2.90 (5th ed. 1988), was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated the trial court imposition of sentence enhancements, holding that the trial court did not instruct the jury that a defendant must be "personally armed" thereby improperly allowing a jury finding of vicarious liability under the enhancement statute. The court vacated and remanded the remaining portions of the judgments that imposed sentence upon defendants. In all other respects, the judgments were affirmed.

Gates v. Municipal Court


No. G011879


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


9 Cal. App. 4th 45; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 439; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1053; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7444; 92 Daily Journal DAR 12025



August 28, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part IV.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 666631, Eileen Moore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: There is no proof in this record that Gates ever intentionally violated any court order or willfully neglected any duty. The superior court did not err in granting the requested writ. Its judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Municipal Court for the Judicial District of Orange County challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which granted respondent Orange County jail sheriff's request for a writ of prohibition and vacated appellant's contempt conviction against respondent for violation of Cal. Penal Code § 827.1, which precluded the pretrial release of persons arrested for certain crimes.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Orange County jail sheriff had to comply with a federal court order that required every prisoner to have a bed or bunk. To make more room, respondent instituted a cite and release program for pretrial arrestees, but was unaware that it violated Cal. Penal Code § 827.1, until appellant Municipal Court for the Judicial District of Orange County brought it to his attention and he amended his policies. Nonetheless, appellant brought contempt proceedings against respondent for intentionally and willfully releasing criminals in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 827.1, at times prior to the revised policy having gone into effect. The lower court found that there was insufficient evidence and vacated the contempt conviction. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that enforcement of § 827.1 was legally impossible while simultaneously following the federal court order to the extent that the cite and release program was necessary to implement the court order. Even if the defense of impossibility didn't apply, appellant never proved that respondent acted with the requisite willful intent to support a contempt conviction. The lower court did not err.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision granting the writ and vacating the contempt judgment. It was legally impossible for respondent Orange County jail sheriff to simultaneously enforce the state law precluding release of persons arrested for certain crimes. Also, appellant Municipal Court for the Judicial District of Orange County failed to prove that respondent willfully neglected any duty.

 

1112 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Laura H.


No. G012021


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


8 Cal. App. 4th 1689; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 285; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1041; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7352; 92 Daily Journal DAR 11923



August 26, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976(b), part II is not published.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. J-411265, Robert H. Keefe, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to California Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the court is directed to hold a new section 366.26 selection and implementation hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, mother of an abused minor and dependent child of the state, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) which allowed the minor to testify in camera regarding her living arrangements decision pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 350(b) without the presence of appellant's attorney.


OVERVIEW: At the selection and implementation hearing of a minor who became a dependent child of the state after her mother's boyfriend abused her, the minor could not choose between living with her maternal aunt and uncle or appellant, mother of the abused minor and dependent child of the state. The trial court proposed that the minor testify in camera pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 350(b) and that only the minor and her counsel be present along with a court reporter, which appellant's counsel did not object to. After reviewing the minor's testimony that she wanted to live with her aunt and uncle, appellant believed that it would have been different had she been present. Appellant challenged the trial court's in camera hearing. The court reversed, holding that the trial court was without authority to exclude appellant's attorney from the hearing, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 350(b), 366.26. While any constitutional right could be waived, mere acquiescence was not a waiver, and there was no showing that appellant was aware of her right to have counsel present at the in camera hearing. Failing to require counsel's presence in the absence of a valid waiver was prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that appellant, mother of an abused minor and dependent child of the state, was entitled to have her attorney present at an in camera testimony of her child on the decision of where she wanted to live. Appellant was prejudiced by the trial court's failure to require her attorney's presence, and the court ordered a new selection and implementation hearing.

Adoption of Michael H.


No. D015795


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


8 Cal. App. 4th 1633; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 261; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1034; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7320; 92 Daily Journal DAR 11844



August 25, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] December 31, 1992, Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1992 Cal. LEXIS 6519.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. A37093, Lisa Guy-Schall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment terminating Mark's parental rights is reversed. The case is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings in compliance with this decision and Adoption of Kelsey S., supra, 1 Cal.4th 816.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which terminated his parental rights, appointed respondent guardians to care for his child, and referred the child for adoption.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father had a child with a woman not his spouse, and after the birth of the child the mother absconded with the child. Appellant attempted to locate the child and, at one point, filed an action for paternity and custody in another state that was dismissed for jurisdictional reasons. Respondent guardians, who had possession of the child, petitioned for adoption with the consent of the mother and also sought a termination of appellant's parental rights. The trial court terminated appellant's parental rights and referred the child for adoption, and appellant sought review. The court reversed and remanded the matter for further proceedings to determine the threshold constitutional question of whether appellant demonstrated a sufficient commitment to his parental responsibilities. The court found that appellant's parental rights were terminated upon a finding that there was no constructive receipt of the child by appellant pursuant to Cal. Civil Code § 7704(a)(4), which had been declared unconstitutional. The court concluded that the parental rights of appellant could not properly be terminated absent a showing of his unfitness as a father.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that terminated the parental rights of appellant father and remanded the cause for further proceedings to determine his fitness as a father. The court found that appellant's parental rights were terminated without the trial court deciding the threshold constitutional question of whether appellant demonstrated a sufficient commitment to his parental responsibilities.

 

1114 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Monterey Peninsula Taxpayers Assn. v. County of Monterey


No. H008155


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


8 Cal. App. 4th 1520; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 188; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1028; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7303; 92 Daily Journal DAR 11800



August 21, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 89619, Robert M. Hinrichs, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a taxpayer association and others, sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California) entering summary judgment in favor of appellee, the taxing authorities, in a suit involving a challenge to the validity of a special tax.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, a taxpayer association, brought suit against appellee, the taxing authorities, challenging the validity of a sales tax that was approved by only a simple majority of voters claiming that Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, or Proposition 13, required that the tax be approved by two-thirds of the voters. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of appellees finding that appellee was not a "special district" within the meaning of § 4 and that the sales tax approval by a simple majority did not violate § 4. The court reversed. The court found that appellee was "essentially controlled" by the county and, therefore, was a "special district" within the meaning of § 4. The court also found that the sales tax was a special tax requiring supermajority approval by the electorate because it was levied for a specific purpose and not placed in a general fund to be used for general governmental purposes. The court also dismissed appellee's argument that retroactive application of the court's finding would cause severe hardship to the county concluding supermajority approval of new special taxes was required regardless of how badly the revenues were needed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment finding that the special tax violated a provision of the California Constitution requiring supermajority voter approval of any new special taxes.

 

1115 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Elguera


No. A054171


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


8 Cal. App. 4th 1214; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 910; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1017; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 7149; 92 Daily Journal DAR 11529



August 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied November 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5622.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Del Norte County, No. 91-030-X, Robert W. Weir, Judge



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Del Notre County (California), which entered judgment convicting appellant of possessing a sharp instrument while confined in state prison, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4502.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of possessing a sharp instrument while confined in state prison, in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 4502. He sought reversal on the ground the lower court omitted the standard instructions on the presumption of innocence and the prosecution's burden of proof beyond a reasonable doubt from its pre-deliberation charge to the jury. The appellate court agreed and reversed the conviction. The court held that although many of the circumstances in the case suggested that the jury, at the deliberation stage, was aware of the presumption of innocence and the prosecution's burden of proof beyond a reasonable doubt, it was not persuaded that the trial court's failure to repeat the necessary instruction in the final charge was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The court concluded that there was a reasonable possibility the omission left one or more jurors without a sufficiently clear definition of "reasonable doubt" against which to measure the doubts that could have been raised by the defense's case.


OUTCOME: The lower court's conviction of appellant for possession of a sharp instrument while confined in a state prison was reversed. The court held that the lower court's failure to instruct the jury on the presumption of innocence and on the "proof beyond a reasonable doubt" standard during trial constituted reversible error because it was impossible to know whether the lack of such instruction contributed to appellant's conviction.

Hung v. Wang


No. B054487


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR.


8 Cal. App. 4th 908; 11 Cal. Rptr. 2d 113; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 980; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6906; 92 Daily Journal DAR 11057



August 6, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III, IV and V.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C753021, Raymond Cardenas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed. The parties on appeal from the judgment of dismissal are to bear their own costs on appeal. The order imposing sanctions on counsel for appellant is reversed. Appellant Sharp is to have his costs on appeal of the sanctions order only.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant shareholder challenged the dismissal of his complaint by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for failure to obtain court approval to file a complaint containing conspiracy allegations against attorneys as required by Cal. Civ. Code § 1714.10, in a cause of action brought by plaintiff against respondent attorneys for conspiracy to loot corporate assets.


OVERVIEW: Appellant shareholder filed a complaint against respondent attorneys alleging that respondents conspired with their clients, also shareholders of the corporation, to loot the corporate assets. The trial court ordered dismissal of appellant's complaint for failure to obtain court approval to file a complaint containing conspiracy allegations against attorneys, as required by Cal. Civ. Code § 1714.10. Appellant sought review, arguing that the § 1714.10 violated the right to a jury trial. The court of appeal affirmed the dismissal of appellant's complaint, finding that § 1714.10, properly construed, did not require judicial factual determinations which infringed on the right to jury trial, and that the statute did not offend the constitutional rights to due process and equal protection of the law. The court of appeal further held that appellant was required to follow the petition procedure specified by the statute before filing an action alleging a conspiracy between an attorney and the attorney's client.


OUTCOME: The court of appeal affirmed the dismissal of appellant shareholder's conspiracy complaint against respondent attorneys because appellant failed to follow the petition procedure specified by statute before filing his action alleging a conspiracy between respondents and their clients.

McClatchy Newspapers, Inc. v. Fresno County Dep't of Social Services


No. F017141


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


8 Cal. App. 4th 855; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 504; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 976; 20 Media L. Rep. 1617; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6867; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10938



August 5, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] February 25, 1993 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at: 1993 Cal. LEXIS 1181.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 71572, Gary S. Austin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting disclosure is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Child Protective Services filed a petition for a writ of supersedeas to challenge a decision of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which granted a request by respondents, newspaper and its reporter, for disclosure of appellant's records concerning two deceased minors.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, newspaper and its reporter, requested release of all records maintained by appellant Child Protective Services pertaining to two deceased minors. Respondents claimed that the information in the minors' records was necessary to serve the public's interest because it showed the manner by which appellant handled cases involving potential child abuse. The juvenile court concluded that it had discretion to release the records to the newspaper, and decided that it would not prohibit such disclosure because the records were directly relevant to an investigation conducted by the newspaper in connection with the administration of a social services program. The juvenile court authorized disclosure of the records, but restricted respondents' use of them. Appellant filed a petition for writ of supersedeas to challenge the disclosure order. The court granted the petition and directed the juvenile court to vacate its order requiring disclosure of the records. Appellant was a public office that administered public social services and received grants by the United States. Therefore, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10850 protected the confidentiality of appellant's records.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the juvenile court's order granting a request by respondents, newspaper and its reporter, for disclosure of records concerning two deceased minors. Appellant Child Protective Services (CPS) was a public office that administered public social services and received grants by the United States, so state law protected the confidentiality of its records.

Antoine v. California Coastal Com.


No. B051709


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX.


8 Cal. App. 4th 641; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 471; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 961; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6737; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10730



July 31, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing August 26, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1045.
Review Denied October 29, 1992 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opinion in the Official Reports( Cal. Const., art. VI @ 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5439.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 153921, William L. Gordon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment granting the writ of mandate is reversed with instructions to remand the matter to the Commission for further proceedings. Costs are awarded to the Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant California Coastal Commission appealed and plaintiffs, homeowner association and individual homeowners, cross-appealed the order of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which stated that the coastal development permit granted to plaintiffs, under the California Coastal Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code, § 30000 et seq., should not contain the stipulation that public access had to be granted along the top of the seawall.


OVERVIEW: Due to severe beach erosion from storms, plaintiffs, homeowner association and individual homeowners, applied to defendant California Coastal Commission for a coastal development permit to build a seawall in front of their homes. Pursuant to the California Coastal Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code, § 30000 et seq., defendant granted the permit on the condition that plaintiffs grant public access along the top of the seawall because the seawall might encroach on state tidelands. The lower court granted a writ of mandate ordering defendant to remove the condition, stating that there was not substantial evidence to support the encroachment finding. Defendant appealed and plaintiffs cross-appealed. The court held that plaintiffs as applicants for a coastal development permit had the burden of proving that the project was to be built entirely on their own land and that it would not have an adverse effect on neighboring property; therefore, the court reversed the judgment granting the writ of mandate with instructions to remand to defendant for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and ordered that the case be remanded to defendant California Coastal Commission because plaintiffs, homeowners association and individuals homeowners, had the burden of proving that the seawall would be built entirely on private land and not have an adverse effect on neighboring property.

People v. Superior Court (Williams)


No. C012131


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


8 Cal. App. 4th 688; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 873; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 962; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6750; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10736



July 31, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing August 28, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 1050.
Real Party in Interest's petition for Review Denied November 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5571.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 96535, James Timothy Ford, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondeat superior court to vacate its order setting aside the peremptory challenge to the Honorable James L. Long, and to enter an order disqualifying Judge Long from presiding in any proceedings in People of the State of California v. Kenneth Derrell Williams, Sacramento County Superior Court No. 96535. Upon finality of this decision, the previously issued stay is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prosecution sought a writ of prohibition restraining respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) from proceeding with defendant's criminal trial before the assigned judge and a writ of mandate directing respondent court to vacate its order disallowing a peremptory challenge to the judge and to issue an order allowing the challenge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder, rape, and other crimes. After two mistrials were declared, a new judge was assigned. Plaintiff prosecution filed a written motion and declaration to disqualify the assigned judge peremptorily pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6. Respondent superior court concluded that plaintiff failed to state articulable facts overcoming a prima facie showing of invidious discrimination. Plaintiff timely filed a petition seeking extraordinary relief. The court recognized that § 170.6 guaranteed litigants, including prosecutors, a right to disqualify a judge. The court concluded that defendant failed to establish the requisite prima facie case of purposeful discrimination; therefore, respondent had no authority to compel plaintiff to assume the burden of demonstrating that the challenge was not predicated on group bias alone. In the absence of a prima facie case of race-based exclusion, respondent was bound to accept the peremptory challenge without further inquiry. Further, the court found, plaintiff's proffered reasons constituted sufficient grounds for the exercise of the challenge.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent superior court to vacate its order setting aside the peremptory challenge to the assigned judge, and to enter an order disqualifying the judge from presiding in any proceedings in the criminal case because defendant did not meet his burden of establishing that plaintiff prosecution exercised the challenge based on race.

Gromis v. Medical Board


No. A054514


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


8 Cal. App. 4th 589; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 452; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 956; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6694; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10636



July 30, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified Auggust 27, 1992. Review Denied October 16, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5101.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 930268, Ralph J. Flageollet, Commissioner.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The parties shall bear their own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff doctor appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied plaintiff's petition for writ of mandate to set aside the suspension of his medical license by defendant, Medical Board of California.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff doctor's license to practice medicine was suspended by order of defendant, Board of Medical Quality Assurance, because plaintiff dated and engaged in consensual sexual activity with a patient. The trial court denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandamus to set aside defendant's order, and plaintiff appealed. The court reversed and held that the trial court's findings were insufficient to support defendant's order of discipline. The trial court made no finding that plaintiff abused his status as a doctor to induce his patient's consent to sexual activity. By all accounts, plaintiff's treatment of the patient's physical ailments was appropriate and competent.


OUTCOME: The denial of plaintiff doctor's petition for writ of mandate against defendant, Board of Medical Quality Assurance, was reversed, and the case remanded for further findings on whether plaintiff took advantage of his status as a physician to induce a patient into a sexual relationship and whether plaintiff's failure to refer the patient for counseling was related to his sexual relationship with her.

Andrisani v. Saugus Colony Limited


Nos. B062500, B064182


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


8 Cal. App. 4th 517; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 444; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 951; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6702; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10546



July 29, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. *]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of section III (entitled Vexatious Litigant) of the Discussion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied October 15, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5147.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. NVC17402 and NVC22251, David M. Schacter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The appeal in No. B062500 is dismissed as moot. In appeal No. B064182, the judgment is affirmed. Appellant shall bear costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property claimant in two consolidated appeals sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed appellant's action to determine his right to possession of certain real property because he had not posted the security previously ordered by the lower court under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 391.1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property claimant filed an action to quiet title to 7.5 acres of real property in a certain county. Respondent interest holders obtained an order requiring that $ 2 million be posted as security, which was reduced following an appeal to $ 42,000. Appellant sought review in the supreme court. When the date for furnishing security to the court the second time passed without being tendered, the trial court granted respondent's motion to dismiss. That order was entered prior to the supreme court issuing its remittitur. Appellant filed a notice of appeal. The trial court noticed the error and set a new hearing after which appellant was given a new deadline to furnish security. When security was not furnished, another order of dismissal was entered. Appellant filed a notice of appeal. One judge entered all of the orders. Appellant had filed a peremptory challenge under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 170.6, but did not bring it to the court's attention. The court held that the first appeal was moot because the trial court had corrected itself. The court affirmed the decision in the second appeal because the peremptory challenge to the sitting judge had not been brought to his attention.


OUTCOME: The court, presented with consolidated appeals, dismissed the first one as moot because the trial court had corrected its own error by resetting the case and issuing another order after the supreme court's remittitur had issued. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in the second appeal, holding that appellant property claimant's peremptory challenge to the sitting judge had been waived by nonaction.
