City of Sanger v. Superior Court


No. A057295


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


8 Cal. App. 4th 444; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 436; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 943; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6606; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10460



July 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 29, 1992. Review of Real Parties in Interest Denied October 16, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5105.
Lucas, C.J., Panelli, J., and Kennard, J., are of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 775865, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ issue directing respondent court to vacate its order granting the motion for judgment on the pleadings and to issue a new order denying the motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review by writ of mandamus of a decision by respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which held that punitive damages were not available to petitioner in an action to recover tort damages from real-parties-in-interest oil companies.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city was one of the plaintiffs in an action against real-parties-in-interest oil companies. The complaint alleged several tort based causes of action arising out of the manufacture, sale, or use of a toxic chemical. Petitioner sought punitive damages. Real parties filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings as to the prayer for punitive damages because municipalities could not recover punitive damages. The motion was granted. Petitioner sought review by writ of mandamus. The court issued a peremptory writ directing respondent court to vacate its order. The court held that the unambiguous language of Cal. Civ. Code § 3294 did not restrict the meaning of the word "plaintiff" when defining who could recover punitive damages, and the lack of symmetry caused by petitioners immunity from punitive damages did not raise constitutional equal protections concerns because the distinction had a rational basis.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ directing respondent court to vacate its order granting real-parties-in-interest oil companies motion for judgment on the pleadings because there was no basis for distinguishing between private and public entity entitlement to punitive damages even though petitioner city could not be liable, and immunity did not raise equal protection concerns because it was supported by a rational basis.

 

1123 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Zaring


No. F014606


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


8 Cal. App. 4th 362; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 263; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 933; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6458; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10218



July 22, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part I.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 29063, Howard R. Broadman and John P. Moran, Judges. * 


* Judge Broadman was the sentencing judge. Judge Moran heard the motion to suppress. In an unpublished portion of the opinion, the court addressed the order denying the motion to suppress, which it affirmed.



DISPOSITION: Appellant's conviction is affirmed. The sentence is reversed. We remand the case to the superior court for resentencing in accordance with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed the sentence and revocation of her probation by the Superior Court of Tulare County (California) for her conviction of felony possession of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(a).


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was convicted of felony possession of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11350(a), and was given five years probation with the conditions that she enter a drug treatment program and that she not become pregnant during her probation. The trial court also required defendant to report on her progress at a court date and threatened defendant with prison time if she did not appear. Defendant was 22 minutes late for the hearing, stating that an unforeseen consequence required that she take her children to school before court. The trial court revoked her probation and sentenced her to prison. Defendant challenged her sentence and the probation condition that she not become pregnant. The court reversed and found that the pregnancy condition was unconstitutionally overbroad because alternative restrictions could achieve the same goal without infringing defendant's fundamental right to privacy. The court found that the trial court abused its discretion when it revoked defendant's probation, holding that defendant's tardiness was not a willful violation of her probation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence, holding that the trial court's revocation of her probation was arbitrary and capricious and an abuse of discretion and that the condition of her probation barring defendant from becoming pregnant was unconstitutionally overbroad.

 

1124 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Arturo A.


No. D014366


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


8 Cal. App. 4th 229; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 131; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 916; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6369; 92 Daily Journal DAR 10061



July 20, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified August 17, 1992.
Review Denied October 29, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5456.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J154487, Michael J. Imhoff, Juvenile Court Referee.



DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss the appeal is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which terminated appellant's parental rights to her minor son, asserting that the standard of evidence used at the hearing, under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26, was incorrect and that she received ineffective assistance of counsel.


OVERVIEW: The lower court determined during a Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.22 hearing that return of the minor son to appellant mother would present a substantial risk of detriment and that there was no substantial probability that the minor could be returned to appellant's custody within a six- month period. At a subsequent selection and implementation hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26, appellant's parental rights were terminated. On appeal the court granted respondent social services agency's motion to dismiss the appeal and found that review of an order terminating reunification services and referring the case to a § 366.26 selection and implementation hearing must be undertaken by petition for extraordinary writ, and could not be mounted as an appeal from the later judgment rendered at the selection and implementation hearing. The court found that appellant failed to offer evidence to support her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel because appellant did not prove that counsel's failure to timely file a review petition was due to negligence and that a timely filed petition would have resulted in a more favorable outcome.


OUTCOME: The court granted respondent social services agency's motion to dismiss the review of appellant mother's termination of her parental rights because appellant's claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was not supported by evidence of negligence or that a more favorable outcome would have resulted. Appellant's claims of errors in her reunification hearing with her minor son were barred because they were not timely brought by writ petition.

 

1125 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Davis


No. D012079


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


8 Cal. App. 4th 28; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 381; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 904; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6349; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9963



July 16, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication.1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part A (2) and B.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. ECR1894, James A. Malkus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgments from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), convicting them of robbery that involved a great taking and of being armed with a firearm in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 211, 12022.6(b), and 12022(a). Defendants argued that the trial court erred in failing sua sponte to instruct the jury according to Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 17.01.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of robbery that involved a great taking and being armed with a firearm. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court rejected the argument that Cal. Jury Instructions - Criminal No. 17.01 was required by the possibility that jurors could split on what defendants did, with some believing they directly participated in the crime and others concluding they were only aiders and abettors. The court held that when conviction on a single count could be based on two or more discrete criminal events, No. 17.01 would be an appropriate jury instruction, and jurors should all agree that defendants were responsible for the same discrete criminal event. Where multiple theories or acts could form the basis of a guilty verdict on one discrete criminal event, however, the charge urged by defendants would be inappropriate. Because both a perpetrator and an aider and abettor would have contributed to but a single criminal event, jurors did not need to unanimously agree on defendants' role, even when different evidence and facts supported each conclusion. The trial court was not required to instruct on jury unanimity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for robbery that involved a great taking and being armed with a firearm because it was not necessary for the jurors to have agreed on the theory of criminality or the theory of criminal participation.

 

1126 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


In re Reylene A.


No. B058055


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SIX.


7 Cal. App. 4th 1822; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 121; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 887; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6145; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9704



July 10, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] October 29, 1992, Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI @ 4; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 5462.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. J42238, Melinda A. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order terminating parental rights is reversed, and the matter is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the order of the Superior Court, Ventura County (California), terminating her parental rights in action brought by respondent Public Social Services Agency (California).


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother's parental rights in her minor daughter were terminated in an action brought by respondent Public Social Services Agency (California). Appellant sought review, and the court reversed, finding that the permanency hearing held pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26, which determined the likelihood of adoption and the best interests of the child, only required the preponderance of the evidence standard and did not address appellant's fitness as a parent, and thus was insufficient to satisfy due process. Moreover, the court found that the finding of clear and convincing evidence of detriment to the child or unfitness made at the jurisdictional hearing dictating temporary removal of the child was insufficient to satisfy due process because it was merely a finding for temporary removal. The court found that, before appellant's parental rights could be terminated, due process required respondent to support its allegations by clear and convincing evidence at the time of the order permanently severing the relationship.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order terminating appellant mother's parental rights and remanded for further proceedings, because respondent Public Social Services Agency was required to show unfitness by clear and convincing evidence.

Mallett v. Superior Court


3 Civil C009144


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 879; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9666



July 9, 1992, Decided.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] ORIGINAL proceedings of the Superior Court of the County of San Joaquin, Judge Reginald Littrell, assigned. Super.Ct.No. 212503.



DISPOSITION: Petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, municipal court judges, sought rehearing of a judgment from the court that affirmed the order from respondent, the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which denied them summary judgment in an action for declaratory relief brought by respondent, a county marshal, contending that the judges exceeded the scope of their authority under Cal. Gov't Code § 68073 when they relieved him of his duties.


OVERVIEW: The marshal brought an action seeking a declaration that he be permitted to perform his elected duties as marshal. The trial court denied the motion by the municipal court judges for summary judgment. The court affirmed the order. The municipal court judges sought rehearing, arguing that the public interest exception to the mootness doctrine was inapplicable because the marshal did not seek a broad declaration as to the overall validity of Cal. Gov't Code § 68073, but merely a declaration that he be permitted to perform his elected duties as marshal. The court denied rehearing, holding that the distinction did not preclude resort to the public interest exception.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for rehearing.

Kees v. Medical Board


No. D012304


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


7 Cal. App. 4th 1801; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 112; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 877; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6163; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9598



July 9, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. 1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III through X.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied September 23, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4693. Rehearing Denied August 3, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 593709, Thomas Oliver LaVoy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant doctor challenged the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that denied his petition for writ of mandate under Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1094.5 to review a decision of Respondent Board of Medical Quality Assurance. Respondent had revoked appellant's license to practice medicine, but stayed the revocation and placed appellant on 10-years probation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant doctor had a long-standing history of alcoholism. Pursuant to an order of respondent Board of Medical Quality Assurance authorized by Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 820, appellant submitted to a psychiatric examination to determine appellant's competency to practice medicine. The examiner reported that appellant was uncooperative during the examination. The court found that appellant had not violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 820 by refusing to take a second psychiatric examination because respondent presented no compelling reason for the request. The court held that the request for a second examination violated appellant's constitutional right to privacy. The court held that respondent had the jurisdiction to prosecute a disciplinary action and that such action was not stayed because appellant was not a formal member of respondent's Diversion Program for Impaired Physicians. The court found that there was substantial evidence to support respondent's action in placing appellant on 10-year's probation.


OUTCOME: The court found that respondent Board of Medical Quality Assurance violated appellant doctor's right to privacy in demanding a second psychiatric examination. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of a writ of mandamus because there was substantial evidence to support respondent's order placing appellant on 10-years probation.

Sandrini Brothers v. Voss


No. F015549


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


7 Cal. App. 4th 1398; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 763; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 874; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 6071; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9549



July 8, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 211751, Stephen P. Gildner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the superior court for further proceedings. Each party is to bear its respective costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant department of food and agriculture challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Kern County (California), which held that Cal. Food & Agric. § 12648 was unconstitutional on its face because it did not expressly provide a means for compensation in the event defendant seized property wrongfully. Plaintiff general partnership challenged the trial court's order denying attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 12648, defendant department of food and agriculture issued a notice of seizure and opportunity to plaintiff general partnership. The notice stated that defendant intended to seize certain grapes prior to a hearing because defendant believed the grapes had been treated with an "economic poison" and would be harvested before completion of the hearing. Plaintiff filed a petition for writ of administrative mandamus pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5 seeking a ruling that the seizure was wrongful. The trial court found that § 12648 was unconstitutional on its face and entered judgment in plaintiff's favor. The trial court, however, denied plaintiff's request for attorney fees. Both parties appealed. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court held that § 12648 was not unconstitutional on its face and that § 12648 did provide an adequate means by which the owner of wrongfully seized property could recover compensation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment rendered in plaintiff general partnership's favor and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court held that the statute authorizing defendant department of food and agriculture to seize plaintiff's crops, which defendant believed had been treated with an economic poison, was not unconstitutional because it provided an adequate means for compensation in the event of wrongful seizure.

Graf v. San Diego Unified Port Dist.


No. D013673


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


7 Cal. App. 4th 1224; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 530; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 852; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5902; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9288



July 1, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied July 22, 1992. Review Denied September 23, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4696.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 582786 consolidated with No. 582790, Mack P. Lovett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), that granted respondent San Diego Unified Port District's motion for nonsuit in appellants' suit to prohibit respondent from enforcing San Diego, Cal., Port District Code §§ 4.30(c), 4.35. Appellants alleged, inter alia, that respondent's regulation of mooring and anchorage in San Diego Bay was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent San Diego Unified Port District enacted San Diego, Cal., Port District Code §§ 4.30(c), 4.35. These ordinances, inter alia, established areas where anchoring or mooring was permitted exclusively in Central San Diego Bay. Appellants filed suit to stop respondent from enforcing the ordinances. The trial court granted respondent a nonsuit. Appellants sought review. Appellants argued, inter alia, that the ordinances were unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. X, § 4 and infringed on the public's constitutional right to anchor freely in the state's navigable waterways. Appellants also contended that one anchor area was dangerous. The court affirmed. The court held that state power to control and regulate use of state navigable waterways was absolute when state action was in the public trust. Judicial review of police power looked at whether or not a regulation was reasonably related to furthering public health, safety, comfort, and welfare. The court found that the ordinances were reasonably related to preserving the public interest in the bay. Respondent had determined that free anchorage was detrimental to overall development and public use of the bay.


OUTCOME: Ruling against appellants, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that respondent San Diego Unified Port District's ordinances permitting only designated anchorage and mooring were reasonably related to preserving the public interest in the bay. Boaters did not have a constitutional right to unregulated long-term anchorage in public navigable waters.

 

1131 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Glenos


No. F014804


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


7 Cal. App. 4th 1201; 10 Cal. Rptr. 2d 363; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 842; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5828; 92 Daily Journal DAR 9145



June 30, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 23, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4710.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Madera County, No. 8672, Edward P. Moffat II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction for violation of section 11366.5, subdivision (a) (count II) is reversed and the sentence on that count vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Madera County (California) that convicted defendant of manufacturing methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379.6 (a), and making space available for manufacturing methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11366.5 (a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of manufacturing methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11379.6 (a), and making space available for manufacturing methamphetamine, in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11366.5 (a). The appeals court affirmed the conviction for manufacturing methamphetamine and reversed the conviction for making space available for manufacturing methamphetamine. The trial court properly denied the suppression motion, brought pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, as there was probable cause for the search warrant. There was sufficient evidence to support the conviction for manufacturing methamphetamine, as the facts supported the finding that defendant knowingly permitted the manufacturing to take place on his property. The court held that the evidence was insufficient to support defendant's conviction of making space available for the manufacture of methamphetamine. Based on the evidence presented, defendant could not have been convicted of the violation. The Double Jeopardy Clause forbade a second trial. Thus, defendant's conviction for and making space available for manufacturing methamphetamine was reversed and retrial was barred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the superior court that convicted defendant of manufacturing methamphetamine, holding that the trial court properly denied the suppression motion, as there was probable cause for the search warrant and there was sufficient evidence to support the conviction. The court reversed the conviction for making space available for manufacturing methamphetamine, holding that the evidence did not support the conviction.

People v. Hove


No. D014687


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


7 Cal. App. 4th 1003; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 295; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 818; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5633; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8830



June 25, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [**1] Opinion certified for partial publication. 1 


1 Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of part II.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied September 16, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4603. Rehearing Denied July 10, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR119481, Bernard E. Revak, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an appeal from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of possessing methamphetamine for personal use, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11377(a), possessing marijuana for sale, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359, and placed him on probation one condition of which required him to register with the local police or sheriff in any community in which he was domiciled.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of possession of methamphetamine for personal use pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11377(a) and possession of marijuana for sale under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359. The trial court placed him on probation, one condition of which required him to register with the local police or sheriff in any community in which he was domiciled. Defendant challenged the statutorily mandated requirement as unconstitutional. Defendant contended that such registration violated his right to personal privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § 1. The court held that although the registration requirement did intrude on defendant's right of privacy, it did not substantially burden that right. The court discerned a rational legislative basis for collecting and maintaining information concerning the whereabouts of drug offenders convicted of certain more serious drug offenses. Retaining such information was properly within the exercise of the state's right to enact laws promoting public health, welfare and safety, by permitting local police to keep track of the identities and locations of drug offenders in the community.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that placed defendant on probation. The registration requirement, which required defendant to register with the local police in any community in which he was domiciled, did not violate defendant's constitutional right to privacy. The registration requirement did not unduly burden the right of privacy of a controlled substance offender and was not prohibited as a dual punishment.

People v. Kirk


No. B061114


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


7 Cal. App. 4th 855; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 270; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 813; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5619; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8729



June 24, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Downey Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. 90M09556, Robert Drees, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the order dismissing the appeal and finding the inferior court's order not appealable, and remand to the Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed a decision from the Appellate Department of the Los Angeles County Superior Court (California) that affirmed the trial court's order to strike from the record one of defendant's two prior convictions for driving under the influence of alcohol or drugs in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled not guilty to a misdemeanor charge of driving under the influence of alcohol or drugs in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23152(a), which occurred within seven years of a prior conviction for the same offense. The trial court found one of defendant's prior convictions constitutionally invalid and struck it from his record. The people's appeal was dismissed by the reviewing court because it held that the trial court's order to strike one of defendant's prior convictions was not appealable under Cal. Penal Code § 1466(a)(2). The reviewing court denied the people's petition for rehearing but certified the case to an appellate court, which transferred it. The court reversed the order and remanded the case to the reviewing court holding that Cal. Penal Code § 1466 and Cal. Const. art. I, § 29 authorized the people's appeal of an order by an inferior court that struck an allegation of a prior conviction before judgment. The court also ruled that it was limited to only the issue certified for appeal and could not rule on the constitutionality of defendant's prior conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order that dismissed the state's appeal as unappealable because both the applicable statute and constitution authorized the appeal of an order by an inferior court that struck the allegation of a prior conviction against defendant prior to judgment.

In re Jennifer M.


Nos. D013799, D014369


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


7 Cal. App. 4th 728; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 428; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 793; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5409; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8473



June 22, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted July 20, 1992. Application for Stay and Review Denied August 13, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4124. Opinion filed September 1, 1992 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J501948, Michael J. Imhoff, Juvenile Court Referee.



DISPOSITION: The order from the section 366.26 hearing is reversed, and the cause remanded for a hearing under section 366.26 so the court may consider the ICPC report and evidence by the grandparents in light of the currently existing circumstances and make appropriate findings under section 366.26. The order finding Scott molested Jennifer and terminating reunification services is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) entered in favor of respondent San Diego County Department of Social Services that found he molested his minor child, terminated reunification services, and directed a permanency planning hearing, and an order that found the minor adoptable and terminated his parental rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father challenged orders of the trial court entered in favor of respondent San Diego County Department of Social Services. The court affirmed the order that found appellant molested his minor child, terminated reunification services, and directed a permanency planning hearing. The court reversed the order that found the minor adoptable and terminated appellant's parental rights. The court remanded the cause for another permanency planning hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 with directions that the juvenile court consider the Interstate Compact on the Placement of Children (ICPC) report, Cal. Civ. Code § 264 et seq., take evidence by the minor's grandparents, and make factual findings under § 366.26. The trial court incorrectly excluded evidence appellant proffered at the permanency planning hearing concerning the grandparents that was relevant to the minor's best interests. The court ruled that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 361.3 provided a preference for placing children with their relatives at the earlier dispositional hearing. The court afforded the grandparents the opportunity to present their evidence as potential permanent caretakers for the minor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order entered for respondent San Diego County Department of Social Services that found appellant father molested his minor child, terminated reunification services, and directed a permanency planning hearing. The court reversed the order that found the minor adoptable and terminated parental rights and remanded the cause for another hearing to consider a report, take evidence, and to make findings on potential caretakers.

 

1135 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Berry v. Hannigan


No. A054526


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


7 Cal. App. 4th 587; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 213; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 786; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5335; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8352



June 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 2, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4429.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 672095-3, Demetrios P. Agretelis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which entered judgment in favor of respondent in appellant's action for declaratory relief. Appellant asserted that statutes requiring operators of towing and storage facilities to accept credit cards as payment from the owners of vehicles that had been involuntarily towed were unconstitutional under the legal tender, equal protection, and due process clauses.


OVERVIEW: California statutes required appellant, owner of a towing business, to accept credit cards as payment from the owners of vehicles whose vehicles had been involuntarily towed. Seeking declaratory relief, appellant asserted that the statutes were unconstitutional under the legal tender, equal protection, and due process clauses of the United States Constitution. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondent commissioner. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed, holding that legislation requiring towing companies to accept checks and credit cards did not violate the legal tender provision of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. Next, there was no denial of equal protection of the laws by requiring towing facilities, operating under a legislative aegis, to accept credit cards from vehicle owners whose vehicles had been involuntarily towed. Finally, appellant was not deprived of property without due process of the law because a statutory lien could be statutorily defined and limited. Thus, appellant was not deprived of property; his property interest was a lien requiring possession of the vehicle, and possession had to be surrendered upon presentation of a valid bank credit card.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that denied appellant declaratory relief from California statutes that required operators of towing and storage facilities to accept credit cards as payment from the owners of vehicles whose vehicles had been involuntarily towed, holding that such statutes did not violate the legal tender, equal protection, and due process clauses of the federal constitution.

 

1136 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Taylor


No. E008813


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


7 Cal. App. 4th 677; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 227; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 783; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5415; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8358



June 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 23, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4704. Rehearing Denied July 17, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. RCR 15387, Kenneth G. Ziebarth, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed; remanded with directions for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which set aside a one-count information filed against defendant. The state alleged that the trial court misinterpreted the California Hazardous Waste Control Law, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25189.5(a).


OVERVIEW: The state filed an information against defendant, charging him with violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25189.5(a) after he closed a circuit board manufacturing company and left behind several drums of hazardous waste. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information which was granted. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the term abandonment under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 25189.5 did not require an affirmative relocation of the hazardous waste, but was simply the permanent and intentional surrendering of dominion and control over that waste. The defense of legal impossibility was not appropriate in that defendant had the opportunity to remove the waste despite having been evicted from the premises. The defense of financial impossibility was rejected on the grounds that it was not available, and even assuming that it was, the evidence adduced at the hearing did not support the defense. There was no question that defendant was aware of the actual facts in leaving behind the waste and was therefore acting in a knowing manner. The statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad or void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The order setting aside the information filed against defendant was reversed and remanded for reinstatement of the information, because no legal impossibility existed which warranted the information's dismissal and the statute was not overbroad or void for vagueness.

League of Women Voters v. Eu


No. C013250


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


7 Cal. App. 4th 649; 9 Cal. Rptr. 2d 416; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 791; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5404; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8494



June 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 27, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4403.
Mosk, J., and Baxter, J., are of the opinion the petition should be granted.
Kennard, J., did not participate.


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDING: Petition for Writ of Mandate.



DISPOSITION: We therefore reject petitioners' claim GATPA violates the single- subject rule. Accordingly, the petition for writ of mandate is denied and the alternative writ is discharged. Respondents and real party in interest are to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public interest groups sought a writ of mandate directing respondent state officials to refrain from taking further action to place an initiative on the ballot on the ground that the initiative embraced more than one subject matter contrary to Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner public interest groups sought a writ of mandate directing respondents, secretary of state and the registrars of voters, to refrain from taking further action to place an initiative on the ballot on the ground that the initiative measure embraced more than one subject matter in violation of Cal. Const. art. II, § 8(d). The court denied the petition and the writ, finding that the initiative measure did not violate the California constitution, because when resolving any doubts in favor of the initiative measure, the provisions were reasonably germane to one another or the objects of the enactment. The court was not required to attempt to predict whether each section would actually further the initiative's purpose.


OUTCOME: The petition for writ of mandate was denied. The court found that the initiative measure did not violate the California constitution, because each provision of the initiative was reasonably germane to the measure's underlying purpose.

Smith v. Alum Rock Union Elementary School Dist.


No. H008721


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


6 Cal. App. 4th 1651; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 399; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 729; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4687; 92 Daily Journal DAR 7404



June 2, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 20, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4249.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 647443, Peter G. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting summary adjudication is reversed. Plaintiff shall recover her costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent school district in appellant's claims of age discrimination under Cal. Gov't Code § 12941.


OVERVIEW: Appellant teacher claimed age discrimination under Cal. Gov't Code § 12941 in a suit against respondent school district. The court granted respondent's motion for summary adjudication. Appellant challenged the order and contended that she stated a cause of action and that the statute's exceptions clause was inapplicable because the statute mandating the discrimination, Cal. Educ. Code § 23902, conflicted with federal law in violation of U.S. Const., art. VI, cl. 2. On appeal, the court reversed and held that where provisions of state law conflicted with the Age Discrimination in Employment Act (ADEA), the requirements of the ADEA had to be read into the state law. Cal. Educ. Code § 23902 had to be invalidated to the extent that it was inconsistent with the ADEA.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment for respondent school district in a case brought by appellant teacher claiming age discrimination and held that the Supremacy Clause required that federal law be read into a state law where the state law was inconsistent with federal provisions.

People v. Rocha


No. H008607


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


6 Cal. App. 4th 1533; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 504; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 687; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4610; 92 Daily Journal DAR 7259



May 29, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 3, 1992 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4509.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 16314, Richard M. Silver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's finding on the criminal gang enhancement is reversed. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which found him guilty of being an accessory to an assault with a deadly weapon and imposed an enhancement for gang-related activity.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving a vehicle in which a member of his gang was riding. The passenger challenged a bystander as to his rival gang affiliations, pulled out a gun, and began firing, wounding the bystander. The passenger pleaded guilty to assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant pleaded guilty to an accessory charge. Pursuant to a plea bargain, defendant agreed to have the gang-related enhancement tried by the court. The court found the enhancement to be true, and sentenced defendant to two years for the assault and to a two-year consecutive term for the enhancement. Defendant appealed arguing that the evidence was insufficient to support imposition of the enhancement, and that the enhancement was unconstitutional because it was vague and overbroad. The court reversed in part, applying the test of Cal. Penal code 186.22(f) because there was no evidence suggesting the three predicate convictions were related to the gang, and the court found that the state failed to demonstrate that the predicate offenses were gang-related. The court did not reach the constitutionality issues, but affirmed all other respects of the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The trial court's finding on the criminal gang enhancement was reversed in part because there was insufficient evidence to establish a "pattern of gang activity." In all other respects, the judgment order convicting defendant was affirmed.

 

1140 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Arafiles


No. C011672


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


6 Cal. App. 4th 1467; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 492; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 696; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4640; 92 Daily Journal DAR 7322



May 29, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied September 3, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4501.
Review denied by In re Arafiles, 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4501 (Cal., Sept. 3, 1992)
Writ of certiorari denied Arafiles v. California, 507 U.S. 934, 113 S. Ct. 1321, 122 L. Ed. 2d 707, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 1472 (1993)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Arafiles v. Curry, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 92567 (E.D. Cal., Dec. 11, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: ORIGINAL PROCEEDING: Habeas Corpus



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant petitioned the court for writ of habeas corpus. Defendant, who was convicted of first degree murder and was sentenced to life imprisonment, contended that Cal. Const. art. V, § 8(b), which allowed the Governor of California a 30-day period to review and affirm, modify, or reverse any decision of the Board of Prison Terms, was enacted after he committed his crime, and was therefore, an ex post facto law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and was sentenced to life imprisonment. The Board of Prison Terms (BPT) decided to grant him parole, and the Governor of California reversed the decision. Defendant filed a habeas corpus petition, claiming that Cal. Const. art. V, § 8(b), which allowed the governor a 30-day period to review and affirm, modify, or reverse the decision of the BPT, was enacted after defendant committed his crime, and contending that the law was ex post facto. Defendant also contended that the governor's reversal was ineffective because he failed to timely act. The court held that the governor's authority to review the parole decision commenced on the effective date of the BPT's decision. The court determined that the review authorized by Cal. Const. art. V, § 8(b), and Cal. Pen. Code § 3041.2, was confined to a reexamination and consideration of the administrative record before the BPT. The court held that the provisions were not ex post facto because they merely provided for discretionary review, and did not punish a previously innocent act. The court denied the petition for writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus, holding that a provision which granted the Governor of California authority to review and affirm, modify, or reverse the decision of the Board of Prison Terms, was not an ex post facto provision because it merely provided for discretionary review. The court held that the governor's reversal was timely, pursuant to the applicable statute.

 

1141 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Briseno v. City of Santa Ana


No. G011685


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


6 Cal. App. 4th 1378; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 486; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 676; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4555; 92 Daily Journal DAR 7225



May 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 27, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4407.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 659206, Floyd H. Schenk, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Overcrowding is a serious problem. But a piecemeal solution like that proposed by Santa Ana is not the answer. We are not unmindful of the demand high urban densities place on community services. Nevertheless, we must presume the Legislature balanced the benefits of the statewide standard it adopted against the burdens it might impose on cities such as Santa Ana. Here, state law clearly preempts local regulation. The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant resident sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which found that section 503(b) of the California Uniform Housing Code, adopted pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 17922(a)(1), was unconstitutional and that respondent city was free to legislate its own occupancy standards.


OVERVIEW: Appellant resident filed for declaratory and injunctive relief after respondent city enacted an ordinance that set occupancy standards that differed from those set forth in section 503(b) of the California Uniform Housing Code, a law passed pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 17922(a)(1). Under the state code, appellant's family could live in their apartment, but under respondent's code, they could not. The court reversed a judgment in favor of respondent and held that respondent's ordinance was invalid. The state law clearly preempted the local ordinance. The court found that respondent did not comply with the specific procedure to enact occupancy standards that differed from those set forth in the Uniform Housing Code. The court determined that to the extent the term "family" was part of § 503(b), it could be severed so as to render the remainder of the section valid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment against appellant resident and held that respondent city's ordinance setting occupancy standards was invalid. The state law clearly preempted the local ordinance and respondent did not comply with the specific procedure to enact different occupancy standards.

People v. Hernandez


No. E009831


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO

1143 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Mills


No. E009011


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


6 Cal. App. 4th 1278; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 310; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 665; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4506; 92 Daily Journal DAR 7109



May 27, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 19, 1992. Review Denied August 13, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Inyo County, No. 17595, N. Edward Denton, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Mono Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Inyo County (California), which convicted him of possession of a firearm by a convicted felon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021(a). Defendant contended that his conviction violated the ex post facto law proscriptions of U.S. Const. art. I, §§ 9-10, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of possession of a firearm by a convicted felon in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021(a). The court affirmed defendant's conviction. It found that § 12021(a) was not an ex post facto law proscribed by U.S. Const. art. I, §§ 9-10, and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9, because defendant was punished for a violation of a new statute rather than receiving an increase of punishment for the earlier offense. The court reasoned that the legal consequences of defendant's past conduct were not changed. The court found that the probation department's notification of active probationers only of § 12021(a)'s provisions was reasonably related to the goals of probation and within its discretion; the decision was not arbitrary, and active probationers was a reasonable classification for equal protection purposes. The court concluded that defendant could not show intentional or purposeful discrimination. The court disagreed with defendant's contention that due process required actual notice of the law he was charged with violating because actual knowledge was not an element of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of a firearm by a convicted felon. It held that defendant was not subjected to an ex post facto law and that the county probation department's failure to inform him of the new law did not violate his equal protection and due process rights.

People v. Cortez


No. H008648


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


6 Cal. App. 4th 1202; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 580; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 668; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4483; 92 Daily Journal DAR 6965



May 22, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4061.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 143803, Alden E. Danner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction and sentence entered by the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), contesting the jury instructions and the validity of his sentence which was enhanced pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 667.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of kidnapping, Cal. Penal Code § 207(a), and allegations of two prior convictions were found true. He was sentenced to 13 years in state prison. The court affirmed, holding that an instruction of unanimity was not required for a kidnapping conviction. The court also held that the California legislature's utilization of its procedure for amendments to § 667 was proper, and that the resulting amended statute was valid. The court held defendant lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of § 667.5, because his sentence was not enhanced under § 667.5. Because defendant did not make a showing of discriminatory enforcement of § 667, the court held defendant's equal protection challenge to § 667 failed. The court rejected defendant's assertion that § 667 granted the trial court discretion to make the terms for multiple § 667 enhancements concurrent to each other as contrary to the clear language of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence, concluding that an instruction of unanimity was not warranted for a conviction for kidnapping and that California's sentence enhancement statute was valid.

Sirott v. Latts


No. B054536


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION SEVEN


6 Cal. App. 4th 923; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 206; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 637; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4299; 92 Daily Journal DAR 6777



May 19, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied June 10, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. NEC-63715, Melvin B. Grover, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff doctor appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that dismissed his action for legal malpractice against defendants lawyer and law firm after their general demurrer was sustained without leave to amend because the action was barred by the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff doctor decided to retire. He sought advice from defendants attorney and law firm regarding the purchase of "tail" insurance coverage for medical malpractice. Defendants advised plaintiff that plaintiff need not pay the premium. Thereafter, plaintiff was sued for medical malpractice, and defendants were unable to reinstate his tail insurance through arbitration. Plaintiff was required to retain counsel to defend the medical malpractice action at his own expense. He settled the action by paying an amount in excess of the premium amount he would have paid for the tail coverage, and brought his legal malpractice claim within one year of the settlement, but not within one year of the arbitration award or of his retention of counsel. The trial court applied Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 340.6 and dismissed the claim. The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal because it found that plaintiff sustained injury when he incurred attorney fees, and the cause of action accrued then, more than a year before he filed the action for legal malpractice, and that defect could not be cured by amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement of dismissal of plaintiff doctor's legal malpractice claim based upon the one year statute of limitations, because it found that the action accrued when plaintiff sustained an injury as a result of the negligent conduct of defendants lawyer and law firm by incurring attorney fees to defend himself from a medical malpractice claim, more than a year before plaintiff filed his legal malpractice claim.

People v. Tucker


No. G011795


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


6 Cal. App. 4th 903; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 187; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 636; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4272; 92 Daily Journal DAR 6718



May 15, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 29, 1992.
Review Denied August 13, 1992 and the Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4135.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court of California, County of Orange, Central Orange Judicial District, No. 89CN00155, Gregory H. Lewis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the lower court is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court's Appellate Department (California), which affirmed his conviction for willfully failing to support his minor child pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 270.


OVERVIEW: After a prior determination of paternity, the state prosecuted and convicted defendant for willfully failing to support his minor child under Cal. Penal Code § 270. His conviction was upheld on appeal. The matter was transferred to the court, which reversed his conviction. The court determined that while § 270 did not create two separate offenses, as defendant asserted, the trial court did interpret the statute erroneously. The court held that the trial court erred when it modified the jury instructions based on its interpretation, as it had the effect of creating a conclusive presumption of paternity where a prior determination existed, and defendant had notice of the ruling. The court ruled that such a presumption was unconstitutional and invalid, and so the jury instruction was improper. The trial court erred when it precluded defendant from introducing evidence, including a letter from the child's mother, to show that he reasonably believed the minor child was not his. The failure to allow evidence relevant to defendant's mistake of fact defense was reversible error, and the trial judge should have allowed defendant's jury instruction regarding this defense as well.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction, which was upheld by an earlier appellate decision, for willfully failing to support his minor child, as the trial court erroneously modified the jury instruction to create a conclusive presumption of paternity, and improperly denied defendant from introducing evidence relevant to his defense of mistake of fact.

People v. Woods


No. A055941, No. A056503


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO.


6 Cal. App. 4th 846; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 886; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 631; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 4149; 92 Daily Journal DAR 6707



May 14, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Granted June 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 763. (See 12 Cal. App. 4th 1139)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. C-31256, James F. Moelk, Judge.



DISPOSITION: On the appeal, the order dismissing the information is reversed. On the petition for writ of habeas corpus, the order to show cause is discharged, and the petition is denied as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which dismissed a murder prosecution twice previously dismissed against defendant on grounds that Cal. Penal Code § 1387 barred a third filing absent a showing of excusable neglect by the state.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder, the prosecution of which was twice dismissed; once because a material witness was missing, and again because of a typographical omission in the information charging the offense. Finding excusable neglect by the prosecution under Cal. Penal Code § 1387.1, a magistrate granted the state's motion to file a third complaint. The trial court, on defendant's motion, dismissed the information on the grounds that Cal. Penal Code § 1387 barred a third filing absent a showing under § 1387.1 of excusable neglect on the part of the prosecution, which the trial court determined was not shown. The state appealed, and the court reversed, holding that because defendant was not prejudiced by the prosecution's neglect, and because there was no showing of bad faith on the part of the prosecution, the public policy promoting substantial justice and favoring trial on the merits should prevail. The court noted that the magistrate had not abused his discretion when he determined excusable neglect on the prosecution's part under § 1387.1.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the state's information that charged defendant with murder, on the grounds that the magistrate had not abused his discretion in determining excusable neglect on the part of the prosecution. The court found that defendant had not been prejudiced by the neglect, nor was the prosecution's actions made in bad faith.

City of Needles v. Griswold


No. E008534


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


6 Cal. App. 4th 1881; 8 Cal. Rptr. 2d 753; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 761; 92 Daily Journal DAR 8111; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 5351

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. BCV 006579, LeRoy A. Simmons, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The preliminary injunction is modified by striking paragraph E thereof, which purports to enjoin the appellants "from interfering with the City's continued exclusive control over the equipment, or personal property necessary to properly operate the Needles Municipal Golf Course." As modified, the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant golf course licensees appealed part of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction to respondent city, owner of the golf course, that enjoined appellants from preventing respondent from taking possession of and using respondent's private personal property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant golf course licensees were licensed by respondent city to operate respondent's golf course. The license required appellants to provide personal property necessary to equip, operate, and maintain the course. Respondent, dissatisfied with appellants' operation of the course, terminated the license and, fearing deterioration of the course during the license's 180-day termination period, sought injunctive relief. The trial court granted the injunction, which also enjoined appellants from interfering with respondent's use of all personal property necessary to the operation of the course, including appellants' personalty. Appellant sought review and claimed that respondent's actions in appropriating the personal property owned by them amounted to a taking for public use without compensation in violation of Cal. Const. art I, § 19, and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code §§ 1255.010-1255.480. The court affirmed the injunction, but modified it to the extent that it enjoined respondent from taking possession of appellants' personalty. The court found that the temporary deprivation of appellant's property, pendente lite, taken for public purposes without compensation, violated California law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of an injunction in favor of respondent city against appellant golf course licensees, but modified it to remove that portion which enjoined respondent from interfering with personal property owned by appellants as an improper taking for public purposes without just compensation.

In re Whitaker


No. A057347


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR

* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of all footnotes.

California Teachers Assn. v. Hayes


No. C009444


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


5 Cal. App. 4th 1513; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 699; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 574; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3757; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5982



April 30, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] This case was previously entitled "California Teachers Association v. Huff."


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied July 30, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3957.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 363630, Michael T. Garcia, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. Appellant Honig shall recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) entered judgment for plaintiffs, teachers association and others, in their action that charged that the legislature evaded minimum education funding levels of Proposition 98 by including child care funds as part of education appropriations. Defendants, state superintendent and child care parties in interest, appealed, arguing that the funding minimum was not restricted to local school funding.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, state superintendent and child care parties at interest, appealed the judgment entered for plaintiffs, teachers association and others, in plaintiffs' action charging that the legislature evaded minimum education funding levels of The Classroom Instructional Improvement and Accountability Act (Proposition 98) by including child care funds as part of education appropriations. Defendants argued that the required minimum was not limited to funding for local schools. The court reversed, ruling that the constitutional provisions reserving the initiative power to local voters were construed in light of other provisions which define the scope of permissible local legislation. The court ruled that local voters were precluded from accomplishing by initiative that which was beyond the powers of the local board of supervisors. The court further ruled that Proposition 98 did not alter the state's role in education and the constitution continued to make education a statewide matter, and that it was within the legislature's plenary power to determine how to fulfill the required education funding level and to include therein funds not controlled by local schools.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision that the legislature could not calculate child care funding as part of the minimum education funding requirement adopted by voter initiative. The court ruled that education was a statewide matter subject to the plenary power of the legislature and that it could properly determine the components of the education funding and could include therein funds not controlled by local schools.

 

1151 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Gilbert


No. H007752


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SIXTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


5 Cal. App. 4th 1372; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 660; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 566; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3702; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5811



April 29, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied August 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 4055.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 137381, Charles Gordon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of judgments from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) convicting him of committing lewd and lascivious acts upon two girls in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 288(a) and forcibly committing lewd and lascivious acts upon another girl in violation of § 288(b). Appellant contended the evidence was insufficient, the trial court committed reversible error, and abused its discretion.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of judgments from the trial court convicting him of committing lewd and lascivious acts upon three girls in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 288(a) and 288(b). The court affirmed the convictions and judgments. The court held the evidence was sufficient for the jury to reasonably find, beyond a reasonable doubt, that appellant committed the acts with the intent required by statute. The evidence showed appellant's intent was to arouse the sexual desires' of himself or the victim in violation of § 288. Appellant also used the required physical force pursuant to § 288(b) because the physical force he used was substantially greater than that necessary to accomplish the act. In addition, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to hold an evidentiary hearing on juror misconduct because appellant's assertions raised no more than a speculative possibility of misconduct. Furthermore, there was no error in allowing expert testimony on the characteristics generally exhibited by abused children because the evidence was admitted to rehabilitate the victims' credibility after appellant's vigorous credibility-oriented cross-examination of the children.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and convictions. The evidence established that appellant had the requisite intent to commit the offenses and used the required physical force. Expert testimony on the characteristics exhibited by abused children was admissible to rehabilitate victims' credibility. No error in refusing to hold hearing on juror misconduct because appellant's assertion raised only a speculative possibility of misconduct.

 

1152 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Michael M. v. Giovanna F.


No. A049899


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO.


5 Cal. App. 4th 1272; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 460; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 567; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3678; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5800



April 29, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 8905529, Bruce Van Voorhis, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Municipal Court for the Walnut Creek-Danville Judicial District sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant biological father sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which dismissed his paternity suit against respondents, biological mother and her husband, on the ground that appellant lacked standing under the Uniform Paternity Act, Cal. Civ. Code §§ 7000-7021.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the biological father of a baby born out of wedlock and born after respondent biological mother married respondent husband. Once advised of the birth, appellant told respondents he wanted to participate in the baby's upbringing and contribute to its support. When respondents enjoined appellant from further contact, he promptly filed suit to declare paternity. On respondents' demurrer, the trial court dismissed the complaint. Because respondents were married when the baby was born, respondent husband was the presumed father pursuant to the Uniform Paternity Act (UPA), Cal. Civ. Code § 7004(a)(1). Under the UPA, the trial court held, appellant lacked standing to bring an action either to establish his paternity or to rebut the presumption that respondent husband was the baby's father. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that, as applied to appellant, the UPA's standing provisions were an unconstitutional infringement on his substantive due process right to form a relationship with his child. Noting that appellant had promptly taken steps to preserve his opportunity interest, the court concluded that he should be allowed standing under the UPA.


OUTCOME: Finding that appellant biological father's substantive due process right to a relationship with his child required that he be allowed standing under the Uniform Paternity Act to attempt to establish his paternity of the child, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded for further proceedings.

 

1153 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Parsons Brinckerhoff Quade & Douglas, Inc. v. Kern County Employees Retirement Assn.


No. F014353


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


5 Cal. App. 4th 1264; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 456; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 553; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5719



April 28, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * 


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and III.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 202018, Henry E. Bianchi, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Kern County Employees' Retirement Association (KCERA) challenged a judgment from the Superior Court, Kern County (California), in an action on a mechanic's lien, where the trial court had ordered foreclosure of a mechanic's lien against improved property owned by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Kern County Employees' Retirement Association challenged a judgment ordering foreclosure of a mechanic's lien against improved property that it owned. The lien was recorded by respondent architectural firm. Appellant contended that the provisions of Cal. Gov't. Code § 31452 prohibited an enforced sale of its library property under respondent's lien. The court affirmed. Section 31452, exempting appellant from process of the courts, was unconstitutional when applied to the enforcement of a mechanic's lien by a claimant whose lien rights were guaranteed by Cal. Const. art. XIV, § 3, which guaranteed a lien to respondent. Under the statutes that implemented and governed the exercise of this right, respondent's lien attached to the library property at the time the first labor or material was furnished for the work of construction. Cal. Civ. Code, § 3128.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment ordering foreclosure on a mechanic's lien against improved property owned by appellant county employees' retirement association because the applicable government code section was unconstitutional when applied to the enforcement of a mechanic's lien by a claimant whose lien rights were guaranteed by the California Constitution.

 

1154 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



County of Yolo v. Los Rios Community College Dist.


No. C009970


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


5 Cal. App. 4th 1242; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 647; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 554; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3766; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5888



April 28, 1992, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 63159, James L. Stevens, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment specifies that County is not legally entitled to recover from Districts "administrative costs" for scheduled elections and also uses the term "precinct costs." As explained in this opinion, we have rejected the unhelpful and amorphous labels "administrative costs" and "precinct costs" in favor of addressing each particular cost at issue. Accordingly, we modify the judgment to dispense with these two labels in accord with the views expressed in this opinion. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California) that held that appellant could not charge respondent college district for school district election preparation costs or for the costs of maintaining voter files, establishing and reviewing precinct lines, training poll workers, or maintaining poll sites.


OVERVIEW: Following respondent college district's failure to pay appellant county for billed election costs, appellant filed a claim for damages and a declaration that its election billing complied with the law. Appellant billed respondent for maintaining voter files, establishing and reviewing precinct lines, training poll workers, and maintaining poll sites. Respondent filed a cross-claim to recover prior election overpayments. The trial court held that appellant could not bill respondent for the aforementioned costs and could not charge at all if an election was not held. The trial court held that respondent's cross-claim was barred by the three-year statute of limitations in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 338. The court affirmed as modified, holding that under Cal. Elec. Code § 23524 and Cal. Educ. Code § 5420, respondent could be billed only for actual costs incurred as a result of its participation in its school election, not for election preparation costs. Respondent was not responsible for list of aforementioned costs because these costs would have been incurred regardless of respondent's election involvement. Those costs billed to respondent when no election was held were also improper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, the trial court's order holding that respondent college district was not responsible for the school district election preparation costs or certain other costs billed to it by appellant county because only those actual election costs incurred as a result of respondent's participation in an election could be charged to respondent.

People v. Higgins


No. C010490


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


5 Cal. App. 4th 1052; 7 Cal. Rptr. 2d 409; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 561; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 3620; 92 Daily Journal DAR 5601



April 27, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Granted July 23, 1992 on Court's on Motion, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 3753. (See 9 Cal. App. 4th 294)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. C-42769, Carl F. Bryan II, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a continuous sexual abuse conviction under Cal. Penal Code § 288.5, in respondent state's favor, in the Superior Court of Nevada County (California). Appellant argued that the statute that he was convicted under was unconstitutional because it permitted conviction without a unanimity finding on specific acts of molestation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his conviction for continuous sexual abuse under Cal. Penal Code § 288.5, based on the alleged unconstitutionality of the statute, arguing that it permitted conviction without unanimity on which acts of molestation occurred. The court affirmed the conviction and rejected appellant's argument, explaining that the statute criminalized a course of continuous conduct and not the specific acts of abuse that occurred. The court noted that the legislature enacted the statute to reach those repeated acts of child sexual abuse that were perpetrated by those with recurring access to the victims. The court found that the evidence of prior uncharged acts of molestation upon the victim were sufficient to support a finding that appellant sodomized and orally copulated with the victim. The court further elaborated that where the issue presented to the jury was whether a defendant committed a course of conduct, a unanimity instruction was proper because the issue was not whether a specific act was committed on a specific day. The court concluded that appellant was adequately apprised of the charges against him consistent with due process requirements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for continuous sexual abuse because a unanimity instruction was proper, and notice of charges consistent with due process requirements was provided, as applicable statute criminalized a course of conduct and not specific acts of child abuse.
