Smith v. Regents of University of California


No. A020553


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


8 Cal. App. 4th 1330; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 384; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 49; 92 Daily Journal DAR 680



January 13, 1992, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied February 10, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. App LEXIS 161.
Review Granted March 26, 1992, (S006588), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1510.
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 2 Cal. App. 4th 750.)] (See 4 Cal. 4th 843)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 519977-2, 533997-6, Zook Sutton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), in favor of defendants, university, its regents, and its student association, holding that the mandatory student activities fee imposed on all students was constitutional and that the use of the fee for financial support was not invalid. The court affirmed, the California Supreme Court granted review, and then retransferred the consolidated cases.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff students challenged the judgment in favor of defendants, university, its regents, and its student association, arguing that defendant university had no right to impose a mandatory fee and that the fees violated their rights under U.S. Const. amend. I, because they were forced to belong and to give financial support to an organization that espoused political and religious views. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that defendant university was a public trust with the authority to assess mandatory student activity fees under Cal. Educ. Code § 89300. The court held that a person's rights under U.S. Const. amend. I were violated only when compelled to make contributions to support an ideological cause. Contributions could have been compelled only for activities germane to the organization's purpose. The court ruled that the fees were germane to the goal of educating students, and within defendant university's mission as set forth in Cal. Educ. Code § 66010.4(c). The court found that the funded student activity groups with religious names were cultural, not "religious," so they fostered defendant university's organizational purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants, university, its regents, and its student association, and held that the mandatory student activities fee was constitutional and the use of the fee for financial support was not invalid. Plaintiff students' First Amendment rights were not violated because plaintiffs were not forced to support an ideological cause. The fees were germane to defendant university's goal of educating students.

People v. Fleeton


No. A049758


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE.


2 Cal. App. 4th 421; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 556; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 37; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 248; 92 Daily Journal DAR 330



January 7, 1992, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied March 19, 1992 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opinion in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.), Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1337.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Nos. 133558 and 133754, Jack K. Berman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The convictions for second degree murder are reversed, and the causes remanded for new trial on those charges. The judgments are otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed judgments from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which convicted defendants for second degree murder and kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with the kidnapping and murder of victim. During defendants' trial, the jury was instructed on second degree felony murder with kidnapping as the underlying felony. Defendants were subsequently convicted of the charged crimes. On appeal, defendants contended that the second degree felony murder rule should not have been applied because it had no statutory foundation. On appeal, the court found that there was no statutory basis for the rule, even though the highest state court applied it. Therefore, the court held that it was bound to apply the second degree felony murder rule. However, the court further held that simple kidnapping was not inherently dangerous to human life, because it did not create a high probability of death. Therefore, the court concluded that the jury should not have been instructed on second degree felony murder. Judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded for new trial.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgments convicting defendants for second degree murder and kidnapping were affirmed in part as to the kidnapping charge. However, the judgment convicting defendant's of second degree murder was reversed in part and remanded, because the trial court erred by charging the jury with an instruction as to that offense.

People v. Thomas Shelton Powers, M.D., Inc.


No. A042063


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


2 Cal. App. 4th 330; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 34; 1992 Cal. App. LEXIS 2; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 145; 92 Daily Journal DAR 116

DISPOSITION: he judgment is reversed insofar as it denies the state further injunctive relief. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. The action is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with our opinion. Costs are awarded to the People.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) which determined that defendants' sales were unfair business practices and granted plaintiff injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were found to have acted jointly and severally in violation of the provisions of San Francisco, Cal., Subd. Code § 1385 by selling condominium units designated as moderate-income housing stock at prices that exceeded those statutorily allowed. It was determined that the sales were unfair business practices under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 17000 et seq. The trial court granted an injunction and imposed civil penalties. However, the trial court determined that it had no power to grant relief such as disgorgement or restitution. Both parties appealed. The appellate court reviewed Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 17203 & 17535 and held the statutes provided a trial court ruling on an unfair trade practice the power to order disgorgement and/or restitution as a form of relief ancillary to an injunction. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's decision but reversed and remanded insofar as it denied plaintiff further injunctive relief.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision and held that defendants' sales were unfair business practices but reversed and remanded the case as it denied plaintiff further injunctive relief.

Walton v. City of Red Bluff


No. C007759


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


2 Cal. App. 4th 117; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 275; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1474; 92 Cal. Daily Op. Service 117; 92 Daily Journal DAR 19



December 30, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tehama County, No. 29861, Stanley Young, Judge. * 


* Judge of the Plumas Superior Court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to the trial court to enter a judgment quieting title in favor of Walton. Costs to Walton.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant heir and appellee city both challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Tehama County (California). The trial court held that land once conveyed by appellant's ancestors to appellee for use as a public library was a transfer subject to a condition; while appellee's temporary nonuse of the building for library purposes did not breach the condition, it continued in effect. Hence, appellee owned the land subject to the use restrictions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant heir's ancestors conveyed property to appellee city subject to its continuous use as a free public library. When appellee ceased using it for library purposes, appellant filed an action seeking reconveyance pursuant to the original grants, which provided for reversion to the grantors' heirs if use as a library ended. Appellee contended that it owned the land free of any use restrictions. Both parties appealed the trial court's judgment that the condition remained valid, but had not been breached by appellee's temporary abandonment of the property for library purposes. The court reversed, holding that the conveyance was subject to a condition that created a possibility of reverter in the grantors and their heirs. Enactment of Cal. Civ. Code § 885.020 abolished the reversionary interest and converted it into a power of termination. While appellant failed to timely record his power as required by statute, appellee waived the issue by failing to assert it before the trial court in affirmative defense to appellant's action, and by virtue of appellee's nonuse for library purposes, appellant established a breach of the condition of conveyance and was entitled to quiet title.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded with directions to enter judgment quieting title in favor of appellant heir. The court agreed with the trial court that the conveyance was not a trust but rather a transfer subject to a condition, which converted into a power of termination by operation of law. The court held that the city's removal of all library books established a breach of the condition of conveyance.

 

1192 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Engelmann v. State Bd. of Education


Nos. C008318, C008701


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


2 Cal. App. 4th 47; 3 Cal. Rptr. 2d 264; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1471; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10214; 91 Daily Journal DAR 16090



December 26, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rules 976(b) and 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts II and III.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied March 19, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1349. The request for an order directing publication of the opinion is Denied.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 361906, James L. Long, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Plaintiff shall recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Board of Education challenged the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), that issued a writ of mandamus commanding appellant to refrain from using procedures for selection of instructional materials that were not prescribed by the Administrative Procedure Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 11370.


OVERVIEW: Respondent publisher petitioned for a writ of mandamus and declaratory relief to require appellant State Board of Education to develop procedures and regulations for selection of school instructional materials in compliance with the provisions of the Administrative Procedure Act (APA), Cal. Gov't Code § 11370. Appellant alleged that its constitutionally delegated authority to select textbooks was not subject to the APA because the Education Code exempted respondent from the APA and, if applicable, the APA violated the doctrine of the separation of powers. The court found that respondents' constitutional power was limited to the ultimate selection of textbooks, that the APA was applicable to respondent's development of procedures, and that the state constitution posed no barrier to enforcement of the APA.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court and held that the procedures of appellant State Board of Education for selecting textbooks must comply with the provisions of the Administrative Procedure Act. The court ordered that respondent publisher was entitled to recover its costs.

 

1193 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



People v. Auer


No. C008199


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, THIRD APPELLATE DISTRICT


1 Cal. App. 4th 1664; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 823; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1466; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 10221; 91 Daily Journal DAR 16060



December 24, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1237.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Placer County, No. 1746, James L. Roeder, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Placer County (California), which convicted defendant of possession of oxycodone (percodan), possession of methamphetamine, and driving with a suspended license. Prior to judgment, the trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Following the denial of his motion to suppress evidence, a jury convicted defendant of possession of oxycodone (percodan) and methamphetamine and driving with a suspended license. Defendant appealed, contending that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress evidence discovered during an impound search of his vehicle. Defendant's car was impounded after he was pulled over for driving with a suspended license. Drugs were found while conducting the inventory for the vehicle. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that the impoundment of defendant's car was authorized by Cal. Veh. Code § 22655.5(a). A police officer observed defendant driving with a suspended license. The court rejected defendant's constitutional challenge to the statute because it had the legitimate objective of preventing the offender from reoffending when the officer completed the citation process and departed. The court held that defendant's assertion that the state failed to show the inventory conformed to standard procedures could not be raised for the first time on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of possession of oxycodone (percodan), possession of methamphetamine, and driving with a suspended license. The court held that the trial court did not err in denying his motion to suppress evidence discovered during an impound search of his vehicle because the impoundment of defendant's car was authorized by statute.

 

1194 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



In re Taya C.


No. D014377, No. D014675


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


2 Cal. App. 4th 1; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 810; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1453; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15953



December 24, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Opinion certified for partial publication. * ]


* Pursuant to California Rules of Court, rule 976.1, this opinion is certified for publication with the exception of parts III and IV.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review Denied April 23, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J502690, Elizabeth Zumwalt Kutzner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The petitions for extraordinary writ of mandate and supersedeas are denied. The stay issued by this court on May 9, 1991, is vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents petitioned for extraordinary relief after the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), found that their child would not likely be returned to their custody within six months, terminated reunification services, and set a hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 to select a permanent home for the child. Appellants claimed violation of due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that child of appellant parents was not likely to be returned to their custody within six months, terminated reunification services, and set a hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26 to select a permanent home for the child. Appellants petitioned for an extraordinary writ of relief and contended that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 366.26(k), which allowed review only by extraordinary writ, was unconstitutional because it violated due process and equal protection. Appellants also argued that the findings of the trial court were not supported by substantial evidence. The court denied the petition and found that not only was § 366.26(k) constitutional but there was substantial evidence to support the trial court's findings. The court found no authority that held a petitioner was denied due process by lack of a reply brief, oral argument or petition for rehearing, and thus substantive review by writ application as prescribed in § 366.26(k) complied with due process. Equal protection was not violated because the greater scope of review given to parents of children in foster care was based on the fact that a foster care order was a final decision of the court.


OUTCOME: The petition for extraordinary writ of mandate was denied. The court concluded that the statutory provision that provided for review only by extraordinary writ was constitutional and there was substantial evidence to support the findings of the trial court.

 

1195 of 1449 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Reiner


No. B056250


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


13 Cal. App. 4th 392; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 472; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1455; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15791



December 19, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Granted April 3, 1992 (S024925).
[Reprinted without change to permit tracking pending disposition on review by the Supreme Court (See Preface to Cumulative Subsequent History Table. Previous cite 1 Cal. App. 4th 1111 and 7 Cal. App. 4th 1582.)]


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 731376, Ronald M. Sohigian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the judgment and remand the matter to the trial court, which is ordered to enter summary judgment for the plaintiffs. The plaintiffs are entitled to recover their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, parents of minor children, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which held that they had no standing, and that Cal. Penal Code § 272, which held parents criminally liable for the delinquency of their children, was not unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: State legislature passed Cal. Penal Code § 272, which held parents criminally liable for failing to exercise reasonable care, supervision, and control over their minor children. Appellants, parents of minor children, challenged the statute under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 526a, arguing that the law was vague. The trial court found in favor of respondents, county and city attorneys, stating that appellant did not have standing to file suit, and the statute was not vague. Appellants challenge the ruling, and the court held that appellants had standing, as they were taxpayers within the court's jurisdiction. The court also determined that § 272 did not define the duty that parents had, or what would constitute failure to exercise reasonable care. The court noted that the statute was not definite enough to provide a standard of conduct for those whose activities were proscribed as well as a standard for the ascertainment of guilt by the courts called upon to apply it. The court concluded that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, reverse the judgment and remand the matter to the trial court, with an order to enter summary judgment for appellants.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, and held that appellants had standing to file suit, and state statute which criminalized parents for failing to exercise reasonable care, supervision, and control over their minor children was unconstitutionally vague. The court remand back to the trial court, with an order to enter summary judgment for appellants.

 

1196 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Citizens for Responsible Behavior v. Superior Court


No. E009929


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO.


1 Cal. App. 4th 1013; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 648; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1444; 91 Cal. Daily Op. Service 9995; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15844



December 17, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 12, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1251.
Panelli, J., is of the opinion the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, Nos. 213210 MF and 213221, Charles D. Field, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged. The petition for writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nonprofit corporation sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California) that upheld real party in interest city's refusal to place petitioner's initiative on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner nonprofit corporation presented an initiative to be placed on the ballot which would have prevented real party in interest city from passing any ordinance or law classifying AIDS or homosexuality as a fundamental right or as a basis for defining unlawful discrimination. Real party in interest refused to place the initiative on the ballot, and the trial court upheld this decision. Petitioner sought review, and the court affirmed. A measure may be kept off the ballot if it represented an effort to exercise a power that the electorate did not possess. The ordinance was not only beyond the power of the electorate but was also substantively invalid. The issue of substantive validity involved a determination of the initiative's constitutionality under the equal protection clause. The standard of review employed when reviewing legislation that did not involve a suspect class or fundamental right was the rational basis test. The proposed ordinance here was invalid under this test. Further, the proposed ordinance was designed to encourage discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment upholding real party in interest city's refusal to put petitioner nonprofit corporation's initiative on the ballot because the initiative involved a proposed ordinance that was constitutionally invalid under the equal protection clause and it also promoted private discrimination.

 

1197 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Ross v. City of Yorba Linda


No. G010204


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE.


1 Cal. App. 4th 954; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 638; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1420; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15556



December 16, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 609593, Robert J. Polis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The Rosses' lot was the object of discriminatory spot zoning. The judgment of the trial court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) entered in favor of plaintiff property owners that found that a one-acre zoning restriction on plaintiffs' lot was arbitrary and discriminatory, and therefore unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owners obtained initial approval from the planning commission for rezoning their lot to the zoning category for most of the surrounding parcels so that they could build another house on the property. Plaintiffs owned a 1.117-acre lot that was virtually surrounded by parcels of lesser size. The city council denied the zoning change after neighbors voiced opposition. Plaintiffs brought a lawsuit against defendant city based on, among other things, discriminatory "spot zoning." The trial court ruled in favor of plaintiffs. And defendant appealed. The court held that plaintiffs' lot was the object of discriminatory spot zoning and that defendant's land use restrictions were arbitrary and discriminatory. The judgment of the trial court was affirmed because defendants incorrectly denied plaintiffs' request for rezoning. The court found that defendant's own records reflected that it amended its general plan to reduce allowable density for just five lots, including plaintiffs' lot, after plaintiffs filed their lawsuit. The court held that this was constitutionally impermissible as it isolated plaintiffs as the special objects of legislative action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiff property owners because the record showed that plaintiffs' real property was the object of discriminatory spot zoning by defendant city.

People v. Lashley


No. B051701


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO.


1 Cal. App. 4th 938; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 629; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1429; 91 Daily Journal DAR 15561



December 16, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied March 25, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 1442.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A924343, John P. Shook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of attempted murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187(a), 664, assault with a firearm § 245(a)(2)), civil rights violations §§ 422.6, 422.7, and brandishing a weapon § 417 (a)(2) upon denial of his motion for new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in a court trial of attempted murder, Cal. Penal Code §§ 187(a), 664, assault with a firearm § 245(a)(2)), civil rights violations §§ 422.6, 422.7, and brandishing a weapon § 417 (a)(2). The trial court found enhancement allegations to be true for personal use of a firearm and intentional infliction of great bodily injury during attempted murder and assault with a firearm, §§ 12022.5, 12022.7 and sentenced defendant to state prison. Defendant appealed. Viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the judgment, the court found sufficient evidence from which the trial court could find that defendant harbored the requisite intent necessary to support his convictions, and his blatantly racial epithets, his brandishing a rifle, and his shooting a victim constituted acts of violence and intimidation motivated by racial hatred. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court found that there was sufficient evidence from which the trial court could find that defendant harbored the requisite intent necessary to support his convictions. As it found no reversible error, the court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence.

County of San Diego v. Department of Health Services


No. D014940


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE.


1 Cal. App. 4th 656; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 256; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1393; 91 Daily Journal DAR 14871



December 5, 1991, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County, No. 633708, Timothy W. Tower, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the decision of the Municipal Court for the San Diego Judicial District of San Diego County, (California), which dismissed its complaint against respondent California Department of Health Services for judicial appeal of a civil penalty assessed against appellant for activities at its long-term care facility, for failure to comply with Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1428(c).


OVERVIEW: A civil penalty was assessed against appellant county for patient abuse at a long-term health facility operated under a license issued by respondent California Department of Health Services by appellant. After using the administrative review processes without satisfaction, appellant filed a complaint seeking judicial review. After respondent answered the complaint, appellant did not file an at issue memorandum within the next six months. Respondent therefore filed a motion to dismiss the complaint. Appellant responded by tendering a tardy at issue memorandum and filing a request for relief from the six-month time limitation on the ground of excusable neglect of counsel, relying on Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473. The municipal court denied appellant's motion and granted the respondent's motion to dismiss. The case was transferred to the court, which affirmed the order of dismissal finding that the failure of appellant's counsel to file a timely at issue memorandum, though inexcusable neglect, was not fraudulent and was non-compliant with Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1428(c) which governed the judicial review of the parties' dispute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant county's complaint against respondent California Department of Health Services for judicial appeal of a civil penalty assessed against appellant for activities at its long-term care facility because appellant failed to follow the procedure required by statute to perfect its appeal.

 

1200 of 1449 DOCUMENTS



Rodriguez v. Solis


No. F014133


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIFTH APPELLATE DISTRICT


1 Cal. App. 4th 495; 2 Cal. Rptr. 2d 50; 1991 Cal. App. LEXIS 1386; 91 Daily Journal DAR 14770



December 2, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Review Denied February 26, 1992, Reported at 1992 Cal. LEXIS 2077.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 411582-0, Lawrence J. O'Neill, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. Costs are awarded to respondent.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant auto dealer sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent Director of Development for the City of Fresno to issue zoning permits to erect freestanding signs. Appellant contended that respondent had a ministerial duty to issue the permits since they were within the applicable zoning regulations.


OVERVIEW: After appellant auto dealer successfully submitted an application for a sign, which was approved by respondent Director of Development for the City of Fresno, appellant applied for three more sign permits. Respondent denied these applications on the ground that the signs would not be compatible with the landscaped environment of the freeway. Appellant unsuccessfully sought judicial intervention, and then filed a petition for a writ of mandate, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1085. Appellant contended that the requested permits were within the zoning regulations and that respondent's job in issuing the permits was ministerial and non-discretionary. Appellant also asserted that the zoning ordinance conflicted with a more specific ordinance dealing with placement of signs on business locations and, therefore the more specific ordinance should control, and that the denial of the permits violated U.S. Const. amend. I. Upon review, the court affirmed, holding that respondent's functions were not ministerial because he was required him make several finding before issuing the permits. The court held that the two statutes were constitutional and could be read together.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because respondent Director of Development for the City of Fresno performed more than a ministerial function when issuing sign permits and appellant auto dealer could not use a writ to compel respondent to perform his job a particular manner. The court also held that the two applicable statutes were compatible with each other and did not unconstitutionally abridge appellants' right to free speech.
